Linguistic Definition of Sujud al-Tilawah
Linguistic definition of tilawah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Sujud al-Tilawah linguistically and distinguish the verbal noun 'tilawah' from its homonym meaning 'following'.

Core Idea
Sujud al-Tilawah means prostration due to recitation; 'tilawah' comes from 'tala' meaning to read, not to follow.

This lesson unpacks the linguistic definition of Sujud al-Tilawah as presented in the opening of its chapter in Maraqi al-Falah. The commentator clarifies that the chapter heading exemplifies the grammatical principle of adding the ruling to its cause, and then traces the verbal noun 'tilawah' to its root meaning of recitation. Understanding this linguistic foundation is essential for correctly identifying the cause (sabab) of this prostration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter heading's grammatical structure
The phrase 'Sujud al-Tilawah' is an idafah (construct) where the ruling (sujud) is added to its cause (tilawah). Ibn Nujaym and others explain that this is the original form of idafah: attributing something to its cause.
Remember: idafat al-hukm ila sababih is a key Hanafi usul principle for deriving rulings.
2
Analyze the word 'tilawah' as a verbal noun
The word 'tilawah' is the masdar (verbal noun) of the verb 'tala' meaning 'to recite' (qara'a). The commentator emphasizes this is the stronger meaning.
Masdar forms can indicate the nature of the action.
3
Distinguish from the homonym 'tala' meaning 'to follow'
The verb 'tala' also has another meaning: 'to follow' (tabi'a). Its verbal noun is 'tuluw' or 'tuluww' (like 'uluw' and 'haml'), not 'tilawah'. This distinction prevents confusion.
Linguistic precision is crucial in fiqh: using the wrong masdar could change the ruling.
4
Conclude the definition
Thus, 'Sujud al-Tilawah' is the prostration that is attributed to the act of reciting (specific Qur'anic verses).
The cause (sabab) is tilawah, i.e., recitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود التلاوة
من إضافة الحكم إلى سببه، وهو الأصل في الإضافة لأنها للإختصاص، وأقوى
مصدر تلا بمعنى قرأ وأما تلا بمعنى تبع فمصدر التلو كالعلو، وتلو أيضاً بوزن حمل
Chapter on the Prostration of Recitation. From the addition of the ruling to its cause, and this is the original in addition because it is for specification, and it is stronger. The verbal noun of tala meaning 'he read'; as for tala meaning 'he followed', its verbal noun is al-tuluw, like al-'uluw, and tuluww also with the weight of haml.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the chapter on Sujud al-Tilawah (Prostration of Recitation) in the matn of Maraqi al-Falah, with hashia commentary. The commentator first explains that the chapter heading is an example of 'idafat al-hukm ila sababih' (adding the ruling to its cause), indicating that the prostration is attributed to its cause, i.e., recitation of specific Qur'anic verses. He then clarifies the linguistic origin: the word 'tilawah' is the verbal noun of tala meaning 'to recite' (qara'a). It is distinguished from tala meaning 'to follow' (tabi'a), which yields the verbal noun 'tuluw' or 'tuluww'. Thus, Sujud al-Tilawah is the prostration performed when one recites or hears certain verses of the Qur'an. This linguistic foundation is important as it establishes the cause (sabab) of the act, fara'id, mudarabah, waqf, faraid, wasiyyah, hudud, qisas, diyat, qada, shahadat, jihad, atimah, luqatah, textual_notes, bibliography, other

Key Terms
سجود التلاوة (sujud al-tilawah): Prostration performed upon reciting or hearing specific Quranic verses; the cause is recitation (tilawah).
التلاوة (al-tilawah): Verbal noun of tala meaning 'recitation'.
إضافة الحكم إلى سببه (idafat al-hukm ila sababihi): Grammatical construction where the ruling is attributed to its cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) To recite (qara'a)
C) To prostrate
D) To glorify

Key Takeaway
Sujud al-Tilawah is prostration attributed to recitation, as 'tilawah' linguistically means recitation, not following.

Istihsan and Qiyas: When Juristic Preference Overrides Analogy
Illustrates istihsan over qiyas with fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where Hanafi fiqh applies qiyas versus istihsan, and explain why istihsan is often preferred.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas when a stronger evidence points to a different ruling.

This passage introduces the tension between qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) in Hanafi fiqh. Through two concrete examples, it illustrates that while qiyas provides a default ruling, istihsan may offer a more equitable outcome. The commentator emphasizes that istihsan is not a marginal tool but a frequently overriding principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
Read the two scenarios: (1) a father buying a slave woman for his insane son, and (2) a chain of liability when multiple people fall into a well. Note the qiyas ruling in each.
The first example involves capacity; the second involves tort liability.
2
Compare qiyas and istihsan outcomes
For each example, state the ruling according to qiyas and the alternative according to istihsan. In the first, qiyas says the purchase is for the father; istihsan says it is for the son. In the second, qiyas assigns blood money in sequence; istihsan (another saying) differs.
The text explicitly says 'by qiyas we take' for the first, and recognizes the second as having another view.
3
Determine which ruling is adopted
Note that in the first example the school follows qiyas (بالقياس أخذ). In the second, the text says 'we adopt it' (وبه نأخذ) referring to qiyas, but acknowledges an istihsan alternative.
This shows that qiyas is not abandoned; rather, istihsan is a competing method that sometimes prevails.
4
Grasp the overarching principle
Understand the commentator's point: cases where istihsan overrides qiyas are far more numerous than the reverse. Fakhr al-Islam is quoted saying the rare category is where qiyas is preferred over istihsan.
This underscores the prominence of istihsan in Hanafi usul.
5
Appreciate the epistemological weight
Recognize that both qiyas and istihsan are valid methods, but istihsan is considered a stronger evidence when it is based on Quran, Sunnah, ijma', necessity, or custom. The choice depends on the strength of the underlying evidence.
This is not arbitrary preference but disciplined legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها رجل له ابن من أمة غيره بالنكاح فاشترى الأب هذه الأمة لابنه المعتوه القياس أن يقع الشراء للأب، ولا يقع للمعتوه وفي الاستحسان يقع، وبالقياس أخذ ومنها لو وقع رجل في بئر حفرت في طريق فتعلق بآخر، وتعلق الآخر بآخر فوقعوا جميعاً، فماتوا فوجدوا في البئر بعضهم على بعض، فإن حافر البئر يضمن دية الأول، ويضمن الأول دية الثاني، ويضمن الثاني دية الثالث، فيكون ذلك على عواقلهم فهذا هو القياس وبه نأخذ، وفيها قول آخر : هو الإستحسان وليس المقصود حصرها فيما ذكر قال فخر الإسلام هذا قسم عز وجوده أهـ، وقد أنهيت إلى اثنين وعشرين مسئلة فأما القسم الذي يرجح فيه الإستحسان على القياس، فأكثر من ان يحصى اهـ من شرح المنار للعلامة زين ملخصاً
Among them: a man has a son from someone else's slave woman by marriage, so the father buys this slave woman for his insane son: by qiyas the purchase falls to the father, and does not fall to the insane son; but by istihsan it falls. And by qiyas we take. And among them: if a man falls into a well dug in a road, and clings to another, and the other clings to another, and they all fall, and die, and they find some on top of others in the well, then the digger of the well is liable for the blood money of the first, and the first is liable for the blood money of the second, and the second is liable for the blood money of the third, and that is upon their 'aqilas. This is the qiyas, and we adopt it. And in it there is another saying: it is istihsan. And the purpose is not to confine it to what was mentioned. Fakhr al-Islam said: 'This category is rare in existence.' End. And I have enumerated twenty-two issues. As for the category in which istihsan is preferred over qiyas, it is more than can be counted. End. From Sharh al-Manar of Allamah Zayn, summarized.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a commentary excerpt explaining the Hanafi legal principle that istihsan (juristic preference) may override qiyas (analogy) in specific cases. The commentator gives two examples from the Hanafi school. First: a father buys a slave woman for his insane son. According to qiyas, the purchase is for the father (since the son lacks capacity), but by istihsan the purchase is for the son to benefit him. The text notes that in this instance the school follows qiyas. Second: a liability case involving a well and multiple victims; qiyas assigns blood money in a chain, but another opinion (istihsan) exists. The commentator then quotes Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi stating that the category where qiyas is preferred over istihsan is rare, while the opposite (istihsan over qiyas) is numerous. This underscores the prominent role of istihsan in Hanafi usul. Authority weight: This discussion is from later works (Sharh al-Manar) but reflects the foundational principles of the school.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning based on an effective cause ('illah) derived from a primary source.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departing from qiyas for a stronger evidence (Quran, Sunnah, ijma', necessity, or custom).
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The paternal male relatives who share the liability for blood money (diyah) when a person commits accidental homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the discussion in this passage, which category of rulings is more numerous in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Cases where qiyas is preferred over istihsan
B) Cases where istihsan is preferred over qiyas
C) Cases where both qiyas and istihsan are rejected
D) Cases where only qiyas applies

Key Takeaway
Istihsan is a valid Hanafi source that frequently overrides qiyas, but qiyas remains operative where no stronger evidence overrides.

Ruling on al-Ta'rif: Imitating the Pilgrims at 'Arafat
Definition and prohibition of imitating the 'Arafat standing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on al-Ta'rif and explain why it is considered a prohibited innovation using the analogy with tawaf.

Core Idea
Imitating the 'Arafat standing is a forbidden innovation because it mimics a place-specific worship.

Al-Ta'rif is a practice where non-pilgrims gather on the day of 'Arafah and stand in imitation of the pilgrims at 'Arafat. The Hanafi school rules this as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it resembles a specific worship act tied to a particular place, similar to performing tawaf around the Ka'bah elsewhere. This lesson unpacks the definition, the ruling, and the reasoning behind the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Define al-Ta'rif in this context: imitating the pilgrims' standing at 'Arafat on the day of 'Arafah. Note that the term can have other meanings, but here it is specific.
The author distinguishes this from other uses of the word like perfuming or announcing lost items.
2
Classify the ruling
The text states it is 'not recommended' (mustahabb) but 'disliked' (yukrahu) in the correct view. The commentator explains it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it is an innovation.
The term 'makruh tahrimi' means it is forbidden close to haram, not just disliked.
3
Analyze the proof: analogy with tawaf
The author draws an analogy (qiyas) between imitating the 'Arafat standing and performing tawaf around any building other than the Ka'bah. Both are place-specific acts of worship that cannot be transferred elsewhere.
The reference to Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Kafi strengthens the analogy.
4
Consider the harm and the principle of repelling mischief
The commentary highlights that these gatherings often involve mixing of men and women and other corruptions. The principle 'repelling harm takes precedence over bringing benefit' is invoked.
This is a key Hanafi maxim used to prioritize prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريف وهو التشبه بالواقفين بعرفات ليس بشيء معتبر فلا يستحب بل يكره في الصحيح لأنه اختراع في الدين ولا يخفى ما يحصل من رعاع العامة باجتماعهم، واختلاطهم بالنساء والأحداث في هذا الزمان ودرء المفسدة قوله : (وهو التشبه بالواقفين) هذا المراد هنا، هو ويطلق على التطيب بذي عرف أي ريح طيبة، وإنشاد الضالة والوقوف بعرفات أي تشبيه الناس أنفسهم بالواقفين بعرفات والأولى التشبيه قوله بل يكره في الصحيح) وظاهر أنها تحريمية لأن الوقوف عهد قربة بمكان مخصوص فلم يجز فعله في غيره كالطواف، ونحوه ألا ترى أنه لا يجوز الطواف حول مسجد أو بيت سوى الكعبة تشبهاً كما في غاية البيان، وفي الكافي من طاف بمسجد سوى الكعبة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ. قوله : (لأنه اختراع في الدين) إذ لم يثبت عنه ، ولا عن أصحابه رضوان الله تعالى عليهم، وما نقل عن ابن عباس أنه فعل ذلك بالبصرة يحمل على أنه خرج للاستسقاء ونحوه لا للتشبيه بأهل عرفات قال عطاء الخراساني : إن استطعت أن تخلو بنفسك عشية عرفة فافعل .اهـ. قوله: (رعاع) قال في القاموس : الرعاع كسحاب الأحداث والطغام وكسحابة النعامة، ومن لا فؤاد له، ولا عقل اهـ، وقال في مادة حدث، والأحداث أمطار أول السنة ورجل حدث السن وحديثها بين الحداثة، والحدوثة فتى والحديث الجديد والخبر اهـ والمناسب هنا هو أرادة من لا فؤاد له ولا عقل وعليه فالمناسب أن يقول رعاعة العامة أي من لا عقل له منهم، والمراد بالأحداث هنا الفتيان أي الشبان قوله : ( ودرء المفسدة مقدم) أي دفع المفسدة مقدم على جلب المصلحة قال في الشرح بعد ذكر هذه العبارة وحسم ذلك الواجب .اهـ.
And al-Ta'rif, which is imitating those standing at 'Arafat, is not something considered, so it is not recommended but rather disliked in the correct view because it is an innovation in the religion. And the mischief that occurs from the riffraff of the commoners by their gathering and mixing with women and young men in this time is not hidden, and repelling harm takes precedence. He said: (which is imitating those standing) this is what is meant here, and it is also applied to perfume with a pleasant scent, and announcing a lost thing, and standing at 'Arafat, i.e., people resembling those standing at 'Arafat, and the better is resembling? He said: (rather disliked in the correct) and its apparent meaning is that it is prohibition-tending because standing was established as an act of worship in a specific place, so it is not permissible to do it elsewhere, like Tawaf and similar. Do you not see that it is not permissible to circumambulate a masjid or house other than the Ka'bah in imitation? As in Ghayat al-Bayan, and in al-Kafi: whoever circumambulates a masjid other than the Ka'bah, it is feared for him disbelief. He said: (because it is an innovation in the religion) since it is not established from him nor from his Companions. And what is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas that he did that in Basra is interpreted that he went out for rain prayer etc., not for imitating the people of 'Arafat. 'Ata' al-Khurasani said: If you can be alone with yourself on the evening of 'Arafat, do so. He said: (riffraff) He said in al-Qamus: al-ru'ā' is like clouds? the young and the rabble, and like a cloud of the ostrich, and those who have no heart nor mind. And he said in the chapter of hadath: al-ahdāth are the rains of the beginning of the year, and a young man, etc. So the appropriate here is those who have no heart nor mind. And accordingly it is appropriate to say ru'ā'at al-'āmmah i.e., those among them who have no reason. And by al-ahdāth here is meant the youths, i.e., young men. He said: (and repelling harm takes precedence) i.e., repelling harm takes precedence over bringing benefit. He said in the Sharh after mentioning this phrase: and preventing that is obligatory.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya addresses the practice known as al-Ta'rif, which was the commoners imitating the standing of pilgrims at 'Arafat on the day of 'Arafah. The author declares that this practice is not considered legitimate; it is not mustahabb (recommended), but rather makruh (disliked) in the correct view, and in fact it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it resembles a specific act of worship reserved for the hajj. The reasoning is that standing at 'Arafat is a rite with a specific location, and performing something similar elsewhere is akin to circumambulating something other than the Ka'bah, which is a form of innovation. The commentary explains that the term al-Ta'rif can also refer to perfuming or searching for a lost item, but here it specifically means imitating the pilgrims. The author cites that the practice is an innovation in religion because it is not proven from the Prophet or his Companions. A report that Ibn Abbas did it is reinterpreted as him going out for the rain prayer. The commentary also discusses the harm caused by these gatherings, where riffraff mix with women and youths, and emphasizes that preventing corruption takes precedence over any perceived benefit. This reflects the Hanafi principle that repelling harm is prioritized over bringing benefit.

Key Terms
التعريف (al-ta‛rīf): In this context, it refers to the practice of imitating the pilgrims standing at 'Arafat on the day of 'Arafah, which the author condemns as an innovation.
رعاع (ru‛ā‛): Riffraff, foolish people with no intellect or heart. Used to describe the commoners engaging in al-Ta'rif.
اختراع في الدين (ikhtirā‛ fī al-dīn): Innovation in religion; a newly invented practice not based on the Sunnah. It is the grounds for declaring al-Ta'rif forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a recommended sunnah.
B) It is permissible (mubah).
C) It is makruh and an innovation.
D) It is obligatory for those not on hajj.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ta'rif is a prohibited innovation because it mimics a place-specific worship and leads to societal harm.

Defining Janazah: The Deceased and the Bier
Definition and chapter placement rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of janazah and explain why the chapter on funerals is placed after the chapter on fear prayer.

Core Idea
Janazah refers to the deceased or the bier, used only after death; its rulings are caused by death itself.

In fiqh, precise terminology is essential before discussing rulings. This passage opens the chapter on funerals by defining the central term 'janazah' and establishing the connection between funerals and the preceding topic of fear prayer. Understanding this definition sets the foundation for the detailed rulings that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the word 'janazah' in the text and note its plural form. The author states it can be pronounced with fatha or kasra on the 'jim'.
Fatha: جَنازة; Kasra: جِنازة.
2
Determine the referents
The term applies to two things: the deceased person (al-mayyit) and the bier (al-sarir). Understand that the bier is included because it is the means of carrying the deceased.
Some scholars restrict janazah to the funeral prayer, but Hanafis include the body and bier.
3
Note the temporal condition
Al-Azhari clarifies that the label 'janazah' is only used after death. A living person is never called janazah.
This condition is crucial for legal classifications (e.g., rulings on washing, shrouding, prayer).
4
Analyze the chapter placement
The text gives two reasons for placing funerals after fear prayer: (1) fear may lead to death, so the topics are connected; (2) fear prayer is a pure right of Allah, while funerals involve both divine and human rights, making the sequence meaningful.
This reflects the structural logic of fiqh chapters in Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أحكام الجنائز جمع جنازة بالفتح والكسر للميت والسرير وقال الأزهري : ولا تسمى جنازة حتى يموت الإنسان كما في التبيين، ومجمع الأنهر، ر، وفي التنوير والسابح في البحر إن أمكنه أن يرسل أعضاءه ساعة صلى بالإيماء وإلا لا تصح قوله : الضرورة) أي لضرورة الخوف، والأولى أن يقول للضرورة بلامين قوله (وفرادى) جمع فرد على غير قياس، وهو حال كما أن ركباناً كذلك من الأحوال المتداخلة، أو المترادفة أفاده السيد قوله : (إذ لا يصح الاقتداء) وقال محمد: يجوز قال في الهداية وليس بصحيح لعدم اتحاد المكان اهـ وفيه أنّ الأكثر تصحيحاً اعتبار الاشتباه، وعدمه في حصة الاقتداء وعدمه. قوله : ( ولم تجز صلاة الخوف) أي صلاة القوم إلا إذا تبين للطائفة الأولى غير ما ظنوه قبل أن تتجاوز الصفوف فإن لهم البناء استحساناً أما صلاة الإمام فصحيحة بكل حال لعدم المفسد في حقه كذا في الشرح قوله : (للأمر) وقوله تعالى : وليأخذوا أسلحتهم. قوله لأنه ليس من أعمال (الصلاة أي فلا يجب فيها كما في البرهان، وفيه أنه برد هذا على القول بالندب وأنّ الوجوب ،لعارض، وهو خوف هجوم العدو، ولا يرد هذا إلا إذا جعلناه من واجبات الصلاة قوله: (للتوقي عن المشي) هذه العلة تشعر بالوجوب لا بالأفضلية، ويمكن أن يقال إنما لم تجب صلاة كل خلف إمام مستقل لوجود أصل العذر قوله : (ونعم الوكيل الذي في الشرح، ونعم النصير وهو بالسجع، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم. باب: أحكام الجنائز الأسب من إضافة الشيء إلى سببه، فإنّ وجوب جميع ما يتعلق بالميت بسبب الميت، ولا بد من حضوره ووجه المناسبة بينها وبين الخوف أن الخوف قد يفضي إلى الموت، ومنه يفهم وجه تأخير الجنائز، ووجهه أيضاً بأن صلاة الخوف حق خالص الله تعالى، وهذا فيه مدخل
Chapter of Rulings on Funerals: The plural of janazah (with fatha or kasra) refers to the deceased and the bier. Al-Azhari said: It is not called janazah until the person dies. As in al-Tabyin, Majma' al-Anhur, etc. ... (the marginal notes continue) ... Chapter of Funerals: 'al-asab' is an addition of something to its cause, because the obligation of all that relates to the deceased is due to the deceased, and his presence is necessary. The appropriateness between it (this chapter) and fear is that fear may lead to death, and from this the reason for delaying the funerals is understood. Also, because the fear prayer is a pure right of Allah, and this (funeral) has an element of it.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on funeral rulings (ahkam al-jana'iz). It first defines the term 'janazah': it is pronounced with either fatha or kasra on the 'jim', and it refers to both the deceased person and the bier (the stretcher). Al-Azhari specifies that it is only called janazah after death. The marginal notes include some earlier clarifications about the fear prayer, but the main text then gives the reason for placing this chapter after the fear prayer: because fear may lead to death, so the two topics are connected. Additionally, the fear prayer is purely for Allah's right, while funerals involve both divine and human rights. The chapter title 'Babu Ahkamil Jana'iz' is followed by a grammatical note: 'al-asab' meaning 'the cause' – the obligations related to the deceased are attributed to the deceased as the cause. The passage ends with the author's supplication.

Key Terms
جنازة (janazah): The deceased person or the bier upon which the deceased is carried; the term is used only after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a person called a 'janazah'?
A) While still alive.
B) Only after death.
C) Only during the funeral prayer.
D) When placed on the bier.

Key Takeaway
A janazah is the deceased person or the bier, and this term applies only after death, forming the basis for all funeral rulings.

The Linguistic Definition of Janazah
Linguistic definition and etymology of janazah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'janazah' linguistically, distinguish between its various usages, and explain its etymological basis.

Core Idea
Janazah linguistically includes both the deceased and the bier, rooted in concealment.

The word 'janazah' appears frequently in funeral fiqh, but its precise lexical meaning is often misunderstood. This passage clarifies the term's pronunciation, referents, and etymology, shaping how we apply it in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronunciations
Note that 'janazah' can be read with fatha (janāzah) or kasra (jināzah). Both are valid and carry slightly different connotations according to scholars.
The difference in pronunciation does not change the legal ruling but affects lexical precision.
2
Analyze the referents
Examine the various opinions: some say jināzah (kasra) is the deceased and janāzah (fatha) is the bier; others reverse; still others say jināzah is the bier with the deceased. Al-Azhari adds that it is not called janazah until the deceased is placed on the bier.
The common thread is that janazah involves both the deceased and the bier.
3
Trace the etymology
The root j-n-z means to conceal, so janazah is that which conceals (the body). It also implies a gathering, as the term is used for a group of people assembled for the funeral.
The dual meaning of concealment and gathering enriches the legal concept.
4
Connect to legal usage
Understanding that janazah includes the bier affects rulings on how to carry it, where to pray, and what constitutes proper funeral rites.
The lexical definition forms the basis for 'janazah prayer' and related obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جمع جنازة بالفتح والكسر للميت والسرير وقال الأزهري : ولا تسمى جنازة حتى للميت والسرير أي هما لهما، وقيل بالكسر الميت نفسه، وبالفتح السرير، وقيل بالعكس، وقيل: الكسر للسرير مع الميت، وكل ما أثقل على قوم واغتموا به فهو جنازة من جنز الشيء بجنزه من باب ضرب إذا ستره، وجمعه كما في القاموس والمصباح وغيرهما سميت بذلك لأنها مجموعة مهيأة كما في مسكين، والموت صفة وجودية خلقت ضد الحياة وقيل عدم الحياة عمن شأنه الحياة كما في التلويح.
The plural of janazah with fatha and kasra is for the deceased and the bier. Al-Azhari said: It is not called janazah until... for the deceased and the bier, i.e., both apply to them. It is said with kasra the deceased himself, and with fatha the bier, and it is said the opposite, and it is said: kasra for the bier with the deceased. And everything that becomes heavy upon a people and they are distressed by it is a janazah, from janaza al-shay' yajnuzuhu from the pattern of daraba, meaning to conceal it. And its plural as in al-Qamus and al-Misbah and others: it is named thus because it is gathered and prepared, as in Miskin. And death is an existential attribute created opposite of life, and it is said the absence of life from one who is characterized by life, as in al-Talwih.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage provides the lexical definition of 'janazah'. The word can be pronounced with fatha (janāzah) or kasra (jināzah). It refers to both the deceased person and the bier. Al-Azhari notes that it is not called janazah until the body is placed on the bier. There are several opinions on which pronunciation refers to what: some say jināzah (kasra) for the deceased and janāzah (fatha) for the bier; others reverse; still others say jināzah is for the bier with the deceased. Etymologically, the root j-n-z means to conceal, so janazah is that which conceals. It is also called janazah because it is a collection of people. Death is defined as an existential attribute created opposite of life, or as the absence of life from one who would naturally live.

Key Terms
جنازة (janazah): The deceased person and the bier; also used for the funeral procession
الميت (al-mayyit): The deceased person
السرير (al-sarir): The bier or stretcher on which the deceased is carried

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which pronunciation of 'janazah' is associated with the deceased himself according to one opinion?
A) Al-fath (janāzah)
B) Al-kasr (jināzah)
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The lexical term janazah is versatile, referring to both the deceased and the bier, with the root meaning 'to conceal'.

Positioning and Prompting the Dying: Sunnah Acts at Death's Threshold
Rulings and recommendations for the dying person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on turning the dying person to face the qibla and prompting the shahada, and explain the evidence from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Turning the dying to the qibla and prompting the shahada are sunnah, not obligatory—easing the final moments.

When a loved one nears death, we often feel helpless. Islamic law provides guidance even at this threshold—not as burdensome obligations, but as gentle sunnahs that honor the soul's journey. In this lesson, we explore two key acts: positioning the dying person toward the qibla and prompting the shahada. By studying their proofs, you will learn why the Hanafi school classifies them as recommended rather than required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate in the passage the two actions: turning the dying person (tawjeeh) and prompting the shahada (talqeen). Note that both are described with the phrase 'يسن' (it is sunnah).
The ruling level is sunnah, not fard or wajib.
2
Locate the dalil for each act
For tawjeeh, the passage simply states 'لأنه السنة' (because it is the sunnah). For talqeen, it quotes two hadith: 'Prompt your dying ones with la ilaha illa Allah...' and 'Whoever's last words are la ilaha illa Allah enters Paradise.'
The hadith are ahad (single-transmitter) so their thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'لقنوا' (prompt) indicates a command. In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub unless there is a qarinah (indication) diverting it to recommendation. The qarinah here is that talqeen is not a pillar of death preparation and is meant to ease the dying person, not burden them.
Thus the command is interpreted as sunnah, not wajib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on talqeen
Some scholars outside the Hanafi school consider talqeen wajib due to the apparent command. The Hanafi school maintains sunnah based on the context and the absence of a penalty for omission.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the school. The later authorities follow the view that both acts are sunnah, as reflected in the text's opening 'يسن'.
The passage does not mention a stronger opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسن توجيه المحتضر أي من قرب من الموت على يمينه لأنه السنة وجاز الاستلقاء على ظهره لأنه أيسر لمعالجته و لكن ترفع رأسه قليلاً ليصير وجهه إلى القبلة دون السماء و يسن أن يلقن وذلك بذكر كلمة الشهادة عنده من لقوله : (لقنوا موتاكم لا إله إلا الله فإنه ليس مسلم يقولها عند الموت إلا أنجته النار) ولقوله : من كان آخر كلامه لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنة أي مع الفائزين وإلا فكل للعبد، وحرمة الحق كحرمة صاحبه، وأيضاً أن صلاة الجنازة ليست صلاة من كل وجه، وهي أيضاً متعلقة بعارض هو آخر يعرض للحي في دار التكليف وكل منها يقتضي التأخير عن أنواع الصلاة، فكيف وقد اجتمعت
It is sunnah to turn the dying person, i.e., one who is near death, onto his right side because it is the sunnah. And lying on his back is permissible because it is easier for his treatment, but his head should be raised slightly so that his face faces the qibla not the sky. And it is sunnah to prompt him, and that is by mentioning the word of testimony in his presence, due to his saying: 'Prompt your dying ones with la ilaha illa Allah, for no Muslim says it at the time of death except that it saves him from the Fire.' And due to his saying: 'Whoever's last words are la ilaha illa Allah enters Paradise.' i.e., with the successful ones.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage provides rulings for the dying person. It is sunnah to turn the dying person onto his right side to face the qibla, as this is the established sunnah for sleep and burial. However, if that is difficult, it is permissible for him to lie on his back with his head slightly raised so that his face is toward the qibla. Additionally, it is sunnah to prompt (talqeen) the dying person to recite the shahada, 'la ilaha illa Allah.' This is based on two hadith: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Prompt your dying ones with la ilaha illa Allah, for no Muslim says it at the time of death except that Allah saves him from the Fire,' and 'Whoever's last words are la ilaha illa Allah enters Paradise.' The prompting should be done gently before the death rattle. The text also notes that the funeral prayer is not a prayer in every sense, which justifies certain differences from regular prayer.

Key Terms
المحتضر (al-muhtadar): The person who is dying, i.e., one whom death has attended
التلقين (al-talqeen): Prompting the dying person to recite the shahada
الشهادة (al-shahada): The testimony of faith: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on turning the dying person to face the qibla?
A) Obligatory (fard)
B) Sunnah (recommended)
C) Makruh (disliked)
D) Haram (prohibited)

Key Takeaway
The dying person should be gently turned to face the qibla and prompted to say the shahada—both are sunnah acts that ease the final moments.

Tayammum for the Deceased by a Mahram and the Khuntha Mushkil
Rulings on tayammum for deceased and khuntha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a mahram to perform tayammum on a deceased person without a barrier, and explain the ruling for the khuntha mushkil in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mahram may touch the deceased directly for tayammum because desire is absent; the khuntha mushkil is given tayammum in the apparent narration.

This lesson addresses two distinct rulings within the broader topic of ghusl and tayammum for the deceased. First, it clarifies when a mahram relative can perform tayammum on the deceased without a cloth barrier. Second, it explains the special case of the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), whose sex is unknown and thus requires precautionary treatment. Understanding these rulings ensures that the deceased's body is handled with both respect and legal precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is a mahram performing tayammum on a deceased relative (male or female). The ruling is permissible (ja'iz), and importantly, it is done without a cloth barrier.
The mahram must be someone with whom marriage is permanently forbidden.
2
Isolate the condition for the barrier removal
The text states the reason: 'without desire' (bila shahwa). Because a mahram is allowed to see certain body parts without lust, touching for tayammum is also permitted directly.
This is analogous to looking – if looking is allowed without desire, touching is similarly allowed.
3
Recognize the khuntha mushkil and its challenge
The khuntha mushkil has ambiguous genitalia, so their sex is unknown. This creates a problem: should they be treated as male or female for ghusl/tayammum? The Hanafi school resolves this with precaution.
The term 'mushkil' indicates the ambiguity cannot be resolved.
4
State the two narrations for the khuntha
In the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), tayammum is performed directly on the khuntha without a barrier. Another view places them in a shirt that does not impede water.
The alternative view shows an extra layer of caution, but the relied-upon position is the apparent narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصره عن ذراعي المرأة ولو عجوزاً وإن وجد ذو رحم محرم يمم) الميت ذكراً كان أو أنثى (بلا خرقة) لجواز مس أعضاء التيمم للمحرم ، بلا شهوة كالنظر إليها منها له (وكذا الخنثى المشكل ييمم في ظاهر الرواية وقيل : يجعل في قميص لا يمنع وصول الماء إليه (ويجوز للرجل والمرأة تغسيل صبي وصبية لم يشتهيا لأنه ليس لأعضائهما حكم العورة وعن أبي يوسف أنه قال : أكره أن يغسلهما الأجنبي، والمجبوب كالفحل) قوله : (والمجبوب كالفحل) فليس له تغسيل امرأة أجنبية إلا أن تكون من محارمه فييممها بخرقة قاله السيد أي، ولا يعطي حكم بسبب الجب، وكذا إذا مات النساء بین أما بخرقة أو دونها على التفصيل، وكذا له أن يغسل الصبي والصبية اللذين لم يشتهيا، فالحاصل أنه في حكم الرجال من كل وجه .
His sight from the forearms of a woman, even if an old woman, and if there is a mahram relative, he performs tayammum on the deceased, male or female, without a cloth, due to the permissibility of touching the parts of tayammum for the mahram, without desire, like looking at them from her for him. Similarly, the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) is performed tayammum on in the apparent narration, and it is said: he is placed in a shirt that does not prevent water from reaching him. And it is permissible for a man and a woman to wash a boy and a girl who have not reached desire, because their body parts are not considered awrah. Abu Yusuf said: I dislike that a non-mahram washes them. And the ma'jub (castrated male) is like an adult male. (The commentator says: The ma'jub is like an adult male, so he is not allowed to wash a non-mahram woman unless she is from his mahram, in which case he performs tayammum on her with a cloth, as said by al-Sayyid. He is not given the ruling due to castration. Similarly, if a woman dies, either with a cloth or without, according to the detail. And similarly, he may wash the boy and girl who have not reached desire. So the result is that he is in the ruling of men in every respect.)
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage begins with a commentary on a ruling regarding tayammum for the deceased. The text clarifies that a mahram relative may perform tayammum on the deceased directly without a barrier, provided there is no sexual desire. This is analogous to the permissibility of looking at such body parts for a mahram without lust. Then it discusses the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), stating that in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), tayammum is performed on him/her, with an alternative view that he/she should be placed in a shirt that does not prevent water. The passage then addresses washing children: it is permissible for adults of either gender to wash a prepubescent child who has not yet reached the age of desire, as children's body parts are not considered awrah. However, Abu Yusuf expressed dislike (karaha) for a non-mahram washing them, indicating a tanzihi dislike. Finally, the ruling on the ma'jub (a man whose testicles have been removed) is given: he is treated as an adult male in all matters of awrah and ghusl, so he cannot wash a non-mahram woman unless she is his mahram, in which case he performs tayammum with a cloth. The commentary underscores that despite his condition, he retains the full legal status of a man.

Key Terms
الخنثى المشكل (al-khuntha al-mushkil): A person with ambiguous reproductive organs such that their sex cannot be determined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition may a mahram perform tayammum on a deceased person without a cloth?
A) If the deceased is a child.
B) If the mahram has no sexual desire.
C) If the mahram is male and the deceased is female.
D) If the mahram is the father of the deceased.

Key Takeaway
A mahram may directly touch the deceased for tayammum only when desire is absent; the khuntha mushkil is treated by direct tayammum in the authoritative narration.

Washing Children by Non-Mahram and the Legal Status of the Ma'jub
Rulings on washing children and status of ma'jub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissibility and the intra-Hanafi dislike of non-mahram washing prepubescent children, and explain the ruling that a ma'jub (castrated male) retains full male legal status.

Core Idea
Children before desire have no awrah, so anyone may wash them; a castrated man is legally a full male in all matters.

This lesson continues with rulings related to the deceased and those handling them. First, it addresses washing young children who have not reached the age of desire – a scenario where non-mahram adults may participate, though Abu Yusuf expressed dislike. Second, it clarifies the status of a ma'jub (a man whose testicles have been removed), affirming that he is treated exactly as an adult male in all legal rulings, including the prohibition of touching non-mahram women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is a man or woman washing a prepubescent boy or girl. The general ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) because children who have not reached desire do not have awrah.
The child is defined as not having reached the age of sexual desire (lam yashtahiya).
2
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf disliked (kariha) that a non-mahram washes such children, indicating a makruh tanzihi. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow it absolutely.
The dislike is a matter of caution, not prohibition.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator does not give weight to Abu Yusuf's dislike; the relied-upon position is that it is absolutely permissible.
Thus, in practice, non-mahram washing children is allowed.
4
Analyze the ma'jub ruling
A ma'jub (castrated man) is legally equivalent to an adult male (kalfahl). He cannot wash a non-mahram woman; if she is his mahram, he performs tayammum with a cloth.
His condition does not grant any relaxation of awrah or touch rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصره عن ذراعي المرأة ولو عجوزاً وإن وجد ذو رحم محرم يمم) الميت ذكراً كان أو أنثى (بلا خرقة) لجواز مس أعضاء التيمم للمحرم ، بلا شهوة كالنظر إليها منها له (وكذا الخنثى المشكل ييمم في ظاهر الرواية وقيل : يجعل في قميص لا يمنع وصول الماء إليه (ويجوز للرجل والمرأة تغسيل صبي وصبية لم يشتهيا لأنه ليس لأعضائهما حكم العورة وعن أبي يوسف أنه قال : أكره أن يغسلهما الأجنبي، والمجبوب كالفحل) قوله : (والمجبوب كالفحل) فليس له تغسيل امرأة أجنبية إلا أن تكون من محارمه فييممها بخرقة قاله السيد أي، ولا يعطي حكم بسبب الجب، وكذا إذا مات النساء بین أما بخرقة أو دونها على التفصيل، وكذا له أن يغسل الصبي والصبية اللذين لم يشتهيا، فالحاصل أنه في حكم الرجال من كل وجه .
His sight from the forearms of a woman, even if an old woman, and if there is a mahram relative, he performs tayammum on the deceased, male or female, without a cloth, due to the permissibility of touching the parts of tayammum for the mahram, without desire, like looking at them from her for him. Similarly, the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) is performed tayammum on in the apparent narration, and it is said: he is placed in a shirt that does not prevent water from reaching him. And it is permissible for a man and a woman to wash a boy and a girl who have not reached desire, because their body parts are not considered awrah. Abu Yusuf said: I dislike that a non-mahram washes them. And the ma'jub (castrated male) is like an adult male. (The commentator says: The ma'jub is like an adult male, so he is not allowed to wash a non-mahram woman unless she is from his mahram, in which case he performs tayammum on her with a cloth, as said by al-Sayyid. He is not given the ruling due to castration. Similarly, if a woman dies, either with a cloth or without, according to the detail. And similarly, he may wash the boy and girl who have not reached desire. So the result is that he is in the ruling of men in every respect.)
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage begins with a commentary on a ruling regarding tayammum for the deceased. The text clarifies that a mahram relative may perform tayammum on the deceased directly without a barrier, provided there is no sexual desire. This is analogous to the permissibility of looking at such body parts for a mahram without lust. Then it discusses the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), stating that in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), tayammum is performed on him/her, with an alternative view that he/she should be placed in a shirt that does not prevent water. The passage then addresses washing children: it is permissible for adults of either gender to wash a prepubescent child who has not yet reached the age of desire, as children's body parts are not considered awrah. However, Abu Yusuf expressed dislike (karaha) for a non-mahram washing them, indicating a tanzihi dislike. Finally, the ruling on the ma'jub (a man whose testicles have been removed) is given: he is treated as an adult male in all matters of awrah and ghusl, so he cannot wash a non-mahram woman unless she is his mahram, in which case he performs tayammum with a cloth. The commentary underscores that despite his condition, he retains the full legal status of a man.

Key Terms
المجبوب (al-ma'jub): A man whose testicles have been removed (castrated).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a ma'jub in relation to washing a non-mahram woman?
A) He may wash her directly because he lacks desire.
B) He must use a cloth barrier, just like any other man.
C) He may wash her only if she is his mahram.
D) He cannot be involved in washing at all.

Key Takeaway
Washing prepubescent children is permissible for non-mahram; a ma'jub retains full male legal status in all matters.

Kissing the Deceased: Ruling and Prophetic Proof
Ruling on kissing the deceased with condition and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing the deceased, identify the condition for permissibility (freedom from desire), and cite the prophetic evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Kissing the deceased is permissible with love and blessing, but becomes haram if done with sexual desire.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies a nuanced ruling: kissing the deceased is generally permissible (mubah) when done as a farewell gesture of love and seeking blessing (tabarruk). The commentary grounds this in the actions of Abu Bakr and the Prophet himself, but adds a critical condition: the act must be entirely free from any prohibited desire. Understanding this ruling requires examining both the prophetic proof and the legal rationale behind the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The base text states 'la ba'sa' (no harm), which in Hanafi fiqh indicates permissibility (ibaha). Determine what this ruling applies to: kissing the deceased out of love and tabarruk.
La ba'sa is a common term for mubah, not wajib or haram.
2
Note the essential condition
The commentator adds 'khalisat 'an mahzur' – the kiss must be free from anything forbidden. The explicit condition is that it must not be done with sexual desire (shahwa). Recognize that this condition transforms the ruling from unconditional permissibility to a conditional one.
If desire is present, the act becomes haram, even if the deceased is one's spouse.
3
Examine the prophetic proofs
Two key narrations are cited: (1) Abu Bakr kissing the Prophet after his death, and (2) the Prophet kissing Uthman ibn Maz'un. Analyze how these actions (fi'l) serve as evidence for permissibility. Note that the proofs are ahad (singular) in transmission but the meaning is clear.
The actions of the Prophet (fi'l) are a source of legislation in Hanafi usul, though not as strong as qawl.
4
Understand the refinement (taqyid)
The commentary refines the ruling by adding the condition. Why is desire forbidden? Because marriage ends with death (nikah muntaqit bi al-mawt), so physical intimacy with a deceased spouse is no longer permitted. This shows how legal reasoning (ta'lil) connects the condition to the underlying cause.
This refinement prevents a misunderstanding that the general permission includes all forms of kissing.
5
Apply to contemporary scenarios
Consider a person who wants to kiss their deceased parent or a righteous scholar as a farewell; it is permissible. But a widow must not kiss her deceased husband with romantic intent. This step demonstrates the practical application of the ruling with the condition.
The intention (niyyah) is crucial – love vs. desire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بتقبيل الميت للمحبة والتبرك توديعاً خالصة عن محظور قوله : (ولا بأس بتقبيل الميت) لما روي البخاري عن عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت: أقبل أبو بكر على فرسه من مسكنه بالسنح حتى نزل، فدخل المسجد، فلم يكلم الناس حتى دخل على عائشة، فتيمم النبي ﷺ، وهو مسجى ببرد حبرة فكشف عن وجهه، ثم أكب عليه فقيله، ثم بكى، ولم يفعل ذلك إلا قدوة به لما روى أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجه، والحاكم مصححاً رضي الله عنها أن رسول الله دخل على عثمان بن مظعون، وهو ميت فأكب عليه، وقبله، ثم بكى حتى رأيت الدموع تسيل على وجنتيه وفي التمهيد لما توفي عثمان كشف النبي ﷺ الثوب عن وجهه وبكى بكاء طويلاً، وقبل بين عينيه فلما رفع على السرير قال: طوبى لك يا عثمان لم تلبسك الدنيا ولم تلبسها» ا هـ قوله: (والتبرك) الواو بمعنى فإن تقبيله عثمان للمحبة، وتقبيل أبي بكر الرسول الأكرم ل لهما معاً قوله: (خالصة عن محظور) هذا قيد في الجواز أما إذا كانت لشهوة فحرام ولو زوجة فيما يظهر لقولهم أن النكاح انقطع بموتها لذهاب محله
There is no harm in kissing the deceased out of love and seeking blessing (tabarruk) as a farewell, free from any forbidden (desire). (Commentary: There is no harm in kissing the deceased) due to what Bukhari narrated from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, who said: Abu Bakr came on his horse from his dwelling in al-Sunh until he dismounted, entered the mosque, did not speak to the people until he entered upon Aisha. He performed tayammum of the Prophet ﷺ, who was covered with a striped cloth, uncovered his face, bent over him, kissed him, and wept. And he only did that following the example of what Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and al-Hakim (authenticating) narrated from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that the Messenger of Allah entered upon Uthman ibn Maz'un while he was dead, bent over him, kissed him, and wept until I saw tears flowing on his cheeks. And in al-Tamhid: When Uthman died, the Prophet ﷺ uncovered the cloth from his face and wept a long weeping, and kissed between his eyes. When he was placed on the bier, he said: 'Blessed are you, O Uthman, the world did not clothe you nor did you clothe it.' Commentary on 'tabarruk': The waw means that his kissing of Uthman was for love, and Abu Bakr's kissing of the Messenger was for both. Commentary on 'free from forbidden': This is a condition for permissibility; if it is done with desire, then it is haram, even if the deceased is one's wife, in what appears, because marriage is severed by death, so its material cause is gone.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on kissing the deceased. The base text states it is permissible (la ba'sa) when done for love and seeking blessing as a farewell gesture, provided it is free from any lustful intent. The commentator supports this by citing several hadith: Abu Bakr kissing the Prophet after his death, and the Prophet kissing Uthman ibn Maz'un. The proof is established through the prophetic practice (fi'l) and the actions of the Companions. The commentator then clarifies the condition: if there is any sexual desire, it becomes forbidden (haram), even if the deceased was his wife, because marriage ends with death, thus removing the basis for such intimacy. The ruling emphasizes the importance of intention (niyyah) in determining the permissibility of the act.

Key Terms
التبرك (al-tabarruk): Seeking blessing through the deceased (a pious person).
الوداع (al-wada'): Farewell; indicates the kiss is a final gesture of separation.
لا بأس (la ba'sa): No harm; indicates permissibility (ibaha) in Hanafi fiqh.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire; the presence of which makes the kiss haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes kissing a deceased person haram according to the commentary?
A) Kissing on the lips
B) Kissing with sexual desire
C) Kissing a non-mahram
D) Kissing after burial

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits kissing the deceased as a farewell, but absolutely prohibits it if any sexual desire is present, based on prophetic example and the ending of marital intimacy with death.

Hierarchy of Kafan Responsibility for an Indigent Deceased
Establishes hierarchy of kafan responsibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchical order of responsibility for providing a shroud for an indigent deceased according to the Hanafi school, and apply the proportional division rule among liable relatives.

Core Idea
The shroud of an indigent deceased is a communal obligation, first on relatives in proportion to inheritance, then the public treasury, then the capable Muslims.

When a Muslim dies without leaving any wealth, the community is obligated to shroud and bury him. However, this obligation falls upon specific parties in a strict order. The Hanafi school establishes a clear hierarchy based on the living maintenance obligation (nafaqa) and inheritance shares. This passage outlines that order, including a critical dispute over who takes priority between a former master and a maternal aunt. Understanding this hierarchy ensures that the deceased's dignity is maintained without overburdening any single party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first category of responsible persons
The text states that the shroud is upon those who were obligated to spend on him from his relatives. Note that this is tied to the nafaqa obligation.
Relatives' liability mirrors the living maintenance duty.
2
Understand the proportional division rule
If multiple relatives are liable, the cost is divided according to their inheritance shares. This is analogous to nafaqa distribution.
The proportion is based on the share each relative would inherit, not equal shares.
3
Determine the next category if no relatives exist
If there are no liable relatives, the obligation moves to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), using funds from heirless estates.
The text specifies 'from the wealth of estates that have no legal heir' as the source for bayt al-mal.
4
Identify the final fallback
If the bayt al-mal fails (due to lack of funds or injustice), the obligation falls on capable Muslims who are aware of the deceased.
This is a collective communal duty (wajib kifayah).
5
Note the distinction between deceased and living
For the deceased, it is obligatory to ask others for help; for a living person in need, he asks for himself, as he is capable.
The commentary emphasizes that only the minimum necessary kafan (kafn al-darura) is required when asking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومن) مات ولا مال له فكفنه على من تلزمه نفقته من أقاربه وإذا تعدد من وجبت عليه النفقة فالكفن على قدر ميراثهم كالنفقة ولو كان له مولى وخالة فعلى معتقه وقال محمد على خالته (وإن لم يوجد من تجب عليه نفقته ففي بيت المال) تكفينه وتجهيزه من أموال التركات التي لا وارث لأصحابها (فإن لم يعط) بيت المال (عجزاً) لخلوه من الأموال بمنعه صرف الحق لمستحقه وجهله فعلى الناس) القادرين (و) يجب أن يسأل له) أي للميت (التجهيز من علم به وهو (لا يقدر عليه أي التجهيز (غيره) من القادرين بخلاف الحي إذا عرى لا يجب السؤال له بل يسأل بنفسه ثوباً لقدرته عليه قوله : (وإن لم يوجد من تجب عليه نفقته) قوله : (أو) وجد إلا أنه معسر قوله : (من أموال التركات) أي من غيرها كبيت الخراج والخمس والركاز ولأحدهما الاستقراض من في محله قوله (وجهله) . عطف المغاير بأن كان يدفع إلى غير من عطف السبب، أو من من يستحق جهلاً وفي نسخة وجهته، وهو من عطف المرادف قوله : (فعلى الناس القادرين) أي فيفترض على سائر الناس العالمين به أن يجهزوه، ويكفنوه قوله غيره) بالنصب مفعول يسأل، وظاهر ما في المجتبى حيث قال : فإن عجزوا سألوا له ثوباً أنه لا يجب عليهم إلا سؤال كفن الضرورة لا الكفاية ،در، فإن لم يوجد من يكفن غسل، وجعل عليه الأذخر، ودفن وصلى على قبره، وسأل متعد إلى مفعولين هنا أو التجهيز مفعول له وفيه أنه لم يتحد فاعله مع فاعل الفعل قوله : (لا يجب السؤال نفي الوجوب، وأما الجواز فالظاهر جوازه لأنه من
Whoever dies and has no wealth, his shroud is upon those who are obligated to spend on him from his relatives. If there are multiple persons upon whom spending is obligatory, then the shroud is according to the proportion of their inheritance, like nafaqa. If there is a master (mawla) and a maternal aunt (khala), then upon his emancipator (mu'tiq), and Muhammad said: upon his maternal aunt. If there is no one upon whom spending is obligatory, then it is in the public treasury (bayt al-mal) – his shrouding and preparation from the wealth of estates that have no legal heir. If the bayt al-mal does not give due to inability (being empty of funds) because it prevents the right from reaching its claimant due to ignorance or injustice, then it is upon the capable people (nas qadirun). And it is obligatory to ask for him (the deceased) for preparation from those who know about him and are capable, unlike the living person who if naked, it is not obligatory to ask for him, but he asks for a garment himself because he is capable. (Commentary on 'If there is no one upon whom spending is obligatory'... Commentary on 'or' meaning that a person exists but is insolvent. Commentary on 'from the wealth of estates' i.e., from other sources like kharaj, khums, rikaz, and one of them may borrow from someone capable. Commentary on 'and its ignorance': opposite conjunction meaning that he pays to someone other than the entitled person out of ignorance, or to someone who is ignorant of the entitlement; in some copies 'wajhahu' which is synonymous. Commentary on 'then upon the capable people': meaning it becomes a collective obligation on all the people aware of him to prepare and shroud him. Commentary on 'ghayruhu' with nasb as object of 'yus'alu'; the apparent meaning in al-Mujtaba where it says: if they are unable, they ask for a garment for him, that only the minimum necessary kafan is required, not the sufficient one. Then, if there is no one to shroud, he is washed, given idhkhir (a type of grass) as covering, buried, prayed over his grave, and the verb 'sa'ala' is transitive to two objects here, or 'tajhiz' is the object for the sake of which, and in it there is that its doer does not coincide with the doer of the verb. Commentary on 'la yajibu al-su'al': negation of obligation; as for permissibility, the apparent is that it is permissible because it is... (text cuts off).
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage outlines the hierarchical responsibility for providing a shroud (kafan) for a deceased person who left no wealth. First, the obligation falls on those relatives who would be liable for his nafaqa (maintenance) during life, and if there are multiple such relatives, the cost of the shroud is divided in proportion to their inheritance shares, analogous to nafaqa. This ties the duty to inheritance rights. The text then presents a dispute: if the deceased had both a former master (mu'tiq) and a maternal aunt (khala), the preferred view (Abu Yusuf) is that the mu'tiq bears the cost, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the maternal aunt is more entitled. If no such relatives exist or they are insolvent, the obligation shifts to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), using funds from estates with no heirs. If the bayt al-mal fails due to lack of funds or injustice, the responsibility falls on the capable Muslims (al-nas al-qadirun) who are aware of the deceased. The commentator emphasizes a critical distinction: for the deceased, it is obligatory (wajib) to ask others to provide the kafan if one cannot afford it, while for a living person who is naked, it is not obligatory to ask on his behalf; he asks for himself because he is capable of doing so. This underscores the community's responsibility for the deceased. The commentary also clarifies that only the minimum necessary kafan is required (kafn al-darura), not the complete sufficient shroud (al-kifaya), and that if no shroud is available, alternatives like idhkhir (grass) may be used.

Key Terms
الكفن (al-kafan): The shroud or burial garment for the deceased.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, responsible for welfare and burial of indigent Muslims.
النفقة (nafaqa): Maintenance obligation; the duty to support certain relatives during life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If no relative is obligated to provide kafan for an indigent deceased, who is next responsible?
A) The mosque committee
B) The public treasury (bayt al-mal)
C) The deceased's employer
D) The local charity

Key Takeaway
The kafan of an indigent Muslim is a shared responsibility, first on relatives in proportion to their inheritance, then the state, then the community.

Internal Hanafi Dispute: Mu'tiq vs Khala and the Obligation to Ask
Presents internal Hanafi dispute on priority of kafan obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi internal dispute regarding the priority between the mu'tiq and the khala for providing kafan, and identify the practical implication of the ruling that one must ask for the deceased's kafan.

Core Idea
When an indigent deceased has both a former master and a maternal aunt, Abu Yusuf prioritizes the mu'tiq; Muhammad al-Shaybani prioritizes the khala — the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf.

The passage presents a specific dispute within the Hanafi school: if a deceased indigent person has both a mu'tiq (former master who freed him) and a khala (maternal aunt), which one bears the cost of the shroud? Abu Yusuf holds the mu'tiq is responsible, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds the khala. Additionally, the commentary clarifies the obligation to ask for the deceased's preparation, distinguishing it from a living person's situation. This lesson dissects the evidence and reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
The deceased has a mu'tiq and a khala, and no other liable relatives.
The dispute only applies when both are present.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
The shroud is upon the mu'tiq. The text presents this as the primary view.
The phrase 'wa lahu mawla wa khala fa 'ala mu'tiqihi' indicates the dominant opinion.
3
State Muhammad's position
The shroud is upon the khala. The text adds 'wa qala Muhammad 'ala khalatihi'.
Muhammad's view is recorded as an alternative.
4
Analyze the basis of each view
Abu Yusuf ties the obligation to wala' (clientage), which creates a legal bond akin to kinship. Muhammad considers maternal blood relation stronger.
The text does not provide explicit evidence, but the underlying usul is inferred.
5
Note the additional ruling on asking
For the deceased, it is wajib to ask for his kafan; for a living person, he asks for himself. This underscores the community's duty.
The commentary limits the asking to kafn al-darura (minimum shroud).

This underscores the community's responsibility for the deceased. The commentary also clarifies that only the minimum necessary kafan is required (kafn al-darura), not the complete sufficient shroud (al-kifaya), and that if no shroud is available, alternatives like idhkhir (grass) may be used.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The person who emancipates a slave; the former master.
الخالة (al-khala): Maternal aunt.
الولاء (al-wala'): Clientage; the legal bond between a freed slave and his former master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa, if an indigent deceased has both a mu'tiq and a khala, who is obligated to provide the kafan?
A) The mu'tiq
B) The khala
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when an indigent deceased has a former master and a maternal aunt, the mu'tiq is responsible for the shroud, following Abu Yusuf's view.

The Hierarchy of Obligation for Shrouding the Deceased
Hierarchy of shroud obligation with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of responsibility for providing a shroud according to the Hanafi school and apply it to specific scenarios, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the husband's obligation to shroud a wealthy wife.

Core Idea
The husband must shroud his wife even if she is wealthy; otherwise, shroud falls on relatives by maintenance, then Bayt al-Mal, then the community.

The shroud (kafan) is a communal obligation (fard kifayah), but specific individuals bear responsibility in a defined hierarchy. This passage from Hashiya al‑Tahtawi explains that order: husband for his wife, then those who were obligated to support the deceased, then the public treasury, and finally the community. It also addresses a key intra‑Hanafi dispute: must a husband shroud his wealthy wife? We will trace the evidence and the preferred (mufta bihi) ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling hierarchy
Read the passage to list the categories of persons obligated to shroud a deceased, in order of priority.
The order is: husband (for wife), then relatives who had maintenance duty, then Bayt al-Mal, then the community.
2
Examine the husband's obligation dispute
Focus on the first level: the husband's obligation when the wife is wealthy. Identify the two opinions: majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) vs. Imam Muhammad.
The relied-upon position (al-ashahh) is that the husband must shroud her.
3
Analyze the dalil for the majority
Understand the proof: analogy to clothing during marriage (kiswa) and the principle that the one who benefits (ghunm) bears the burden.
The husband enjoyed financial benefits during marriage, so he remains responsible for preparation at death.
4
Understand Imam Muhammad's reasoning
Imam Muhammad argues that marriage is completely severed at death (inqita' al-zawjiyya min kulli wajh), making the husband a stranger.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school, but the fatwa follows the majority.
5
Apply the hierarchy to a scenario
Given a deceased who has no wealth, a husband, a brother, and a maternal aunt, determine who is obligated and in what order.
First the husband (for his wife), then the brother (relative with maintenance duty), then the aunt (mahram), then Bayt al-Mal, then community.

A wealthy woman (in the soundest view and upon it is the fatwa, and Muhammad said: He is not obligated to shroud her due to the complete severance of marriage). And whoever dies and has no wealth, his shroud is upon those whose maintenance is obligatory upon him from his relatives. And if there are multiple persons upon whom maintenance is obligatory, then the shroud is according to their inheritance shares like maintenance. And if he has a freed slave and a maternal aunt, then it is upon his emancipator, and Muhammad said: upon his maternal aunt. And if there is no one upon whom his maintenance is obligatory, then in the Bayt al-Mal is his shrouding and preparation from the wealth of estates that have no heir. Then if the Bayt al-Mal does not give due to inability due to being empty of funds, due to preventing the right from reaching its due recipient or ignorance, then upon the capable people. And it is obligatory to ask for him (the deceased) for preparation from those who know about him and who are not capable of preparing him from the capable ones, contrary to the living person if he is naked: it is not obligatory to ask for him, rather he asks for himself for a garment because he is capable of it. And if something remains from it, it is returned to its owner; if not known, another is shrouded with it; otherwise it is given in charity. And it is not obligatory upon someone who only has one garment to shroud a deceased who has no other garment. And if a wild animal eats the deceased, the shroud is for the one who donated it, not for the heir of the deceased. And if most of the body is found or half with the head, he is washed and prayed over; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah presents the rulings on who is obligated to provide the shroud for a deceased person. It begins with the case of a wealthy woman: the relied-upon position (al-ashahh wa 'alayhi al-fatwa) is that her husband must shroud her, even though she is wealthy. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the marriage bond is completely severed at death, making the husband a stranger. The Hashiya clarifies that Abu Yusuf has two narrations, but the preferred view is that the husband must shroud her because it is analogous to clothing during her life. The passage then outlines the hierarchy: if the deceased has no wealth, the shroud is upon those who were obligated to support him (relatives). If multiple relatives are responsible, the cost is divided according to inheritance shares. If there is a freed slave and a maternal aunt, the freed slave is responsible (according to Abu Hanifa) while Muhammad says the maternal aunt (since she is a mahram relative). If no such relatives exist, the Bayt al-Mal provides from estates with no heirs. If the Bayt al-Mal is unable, the obligation falls on the community. The passage also discusses the ruling on asking for the shroud for the deceased: it is obligatory to ask those who are capable, unlike for a living person. Additional rulings cover leftover cloth (returned to owner or used for another), the case of a person with only one garment (they are not obliged to give it), and if a wild animal eats the body, the shroud goes to the donor. Finally, if more than half the body or half with the head is found, it is washed and prayed over; otherwise not.

Key Terms
كفن (kafan): The shroud or burial garment used to wrap the deceased.
موسرة (musira): A wealthy woman who has her own property or wealth.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, responsible for expenses of those without support.
رحم محرم (rahim muharram): A relative who is a mahram (permanently prohibited to marry), such as a brother or uncle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a deceased person has no wealth and no relatives obligated for maintenance, who is responsible for the shroud?
A) The community of capable Muslims
B) The Bayt al-Mal
C) The husband
D) The deceased's estate (if any)

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of shroud obligation begins with the husband for his wife, followed by relatives on whom maintenance was obligatory, then Bayt al-Mal, and finally the community, with the husband's duty applying even if the wife is wealthy.

Sunnah Shroud for a Man: Three Garments
Describing the sunnah shroud for a man and its components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three levels of shroud (sunnah, kifaya, darura), list the three sunnah garments for a man, and identify when increasing the number of garments is permissible or disliked.

Core Idea
The sunnah shroud for a man is three emphasized-sunnah pieces: qamees, izaar, and lifafa; increasing is usually disliked unless bequeathed.

Shrouding the deceased is a communal obligation, but what constitutes the ideal shroud? This passage from the Hanafi tradition lays out the sunnah for a man's shroud: three specific garments. It also clarifies the hierarchy of shrouding levels—sunnah, kifaya, and darura—and addresses the ruling on using more than three garments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three levels of shroud
The passage divides the shroud into sunnah (complete), kifaya (sufficient), and darura (necessary). Understand each: sunnah is the ideal, kifaya covers the body, darura just covers the awrah.
Sunnah is the recommended form; kifaya is permissible; darura is the absolute minimum.
2
List the three sunnah garments and their definitions
For a man, the sunnah consists of a qamees (shirt from neck to feet, no sleeves), an izaar (wrap from head to feet), and a lifafa (outer sheet longer than the body). Memorize these and their dimensions.
The qamees is also called dir'; izaar and lifafa are sometimes used synonymously but here izaar is the inner wrap.
3
Note the dalil and the ruling on increasing garments
The sunnah is based on the Prophet's action (shrouded in three). Increasing beyond three is generally makruh unless the deceased bequeathed more, in which case it is not disliked. Some Hanafi scholars allow up to five without dislike.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim: 'the Prophet was shrouded in three garments.'
4
Recognize the communal obligation of shrouding
Takfin (shrouding) is fard kifaya—if some Muslims perform it, the obligation is lifted from the rest. This is the default ruling for burial preparations.
This is the first sentence of the passage: 'And shrouding is a communal obligation.'

And the shrouding is a communal obligation. As for the number of its garments, there are three categories: sunnah, sufficiency, and necessity. The first is: the sunnah shroud of a man is three garments: a shirt from the base of the neck to the feet without a pocket? and sleeves, and an izaar from the head to the feet, and the third a wrapping sheet that extends above the head and the feet to wrap the deceased and tie it.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah shroud for a man. It states that the shroud (takfin) is a communal obligation (fard kifaya). The garments for the shroud are divided into three levels: sunnah (complete), kifaya (sufficient), and darura (necessary). The sunnah for a man is three garments: a qamis (shirt) from the neck to the feet, without sleeves (or without openings?), an izaar (a large cloth) from the head to the feet, and a lifafa (wrapping sheet) that is longer than the izaar to wrap the entire body. The commentary then discusses various rulings: if someone has only one garment, they are not obliged to give it for the deceased; if more than half the body with the head is found, it is washed and prayed over; otherwise it is buried without prayer. The shroud of a child or stillborn is just a cloth, not the full three garments, because they do not have full honor as a 'deceased' person. The number three is based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who was shrouded in three garments, and because that is the maximum a man wears during life. It is disliked to increase beyond three unless the deceased bequeathed it. However, some scholars allow up to five without disliking. The qamis is also called a dir', and the izaar is synonymous with rida' and lifafa, covering from head to foot. The commentary also notes that the preferred size is from the head (qarn) to the foot, though some say from the shoulder.

Key Terms
قميص (qamis): A shirt-like garment from neck to feet, without sleeves (according to the preferred interpretation).
إزار (izar): A large cloth wrapping from head to feet, synonymous with rida' and lifafa in some contexts.
لفافة (lifafa): The outer wrapping sheet that enfolds the deceased, longer than the izaar.
سنة وكفاية وضرورة (sunnah wa kifaya wa darura): The three levels of shroud: sunnah (complete three garments), kifaya (sufficient covering of the body), darura (minimum to cover the 'awrah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is recommended (mustahabb).
B) It is disliked (makruh) according to the relied-upon opinion.
C) It is obligatory (fard).
D) It is permissible (mubah) without any dislike.

Key Takeaway
A man's sunnah shroud is three garments: qamees, izaar, and lifafa; increasing is generally disliked unless the deceased bequeathed more, though some scholars allow up to five without dislike.
