Using Incense and Avoiding Sound at Funerals
Base ruling and commentary on incense, odd numbers, and prohibition of sound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using incense for the deceased and apply the prohibition of following funerals with sound or fire.

Core Idea
Incense for the deceased is sunnah in odd numbers; following a funeral with sound or fire is makruh.

This lesson examines two related funeral practices: the recommended use of incense (tajmir) for the deceased and the disliked practices of accompanying the funeral with sound or fire. We will trace the hadith evidence and the Hanafi juristic reasoning behind these rulings, clarifying what is sunnah, what is makruh, and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
First, identify the two main acts: tajmir (incense fumigation) and following the funeral with sound or fire. Tajmir is recommended (sunnah) in odd numbers, while following with sound/fire is disliked (makruh).
2
Locate the hadith evidence
The commentary cites hadith from al-Hakim and al-Bayhaqi for tajmir (three times, at three moments) and from Abu Dawud for the prohibition of sound and fire. Identify these sources.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
For the prohibition, the hadith 'La tattabi'u...' is a prohibition. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition typically indicates tanzihi (dislike) unless additional evidence raises it to tahrim. Here, the reasoning of resembling the People of the Book (tashabbuh) and pessimism (tafa'ul) confirms the dislike.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis consider the prohibition as makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) rather than makruh tahrimi. Understand the difference in legal consequence.
5
Apply the rulings practically
Apply the rulings: incense is used at three points (death, washing, shrouding) in odd numbers (preferably 3). Avoid any form of sound (wailing, music) or fire (torches, candles) in the funeral procession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الميت فأجمرا وأوترا ولا يزاد على خمس ولا تتبع الجنازة بصوت ولا نار ويكره تجمير القبر قوله: (فأجمرا) وأوترا) وفي رواية للحاكم: إذا أجمرتم الميت فأجمروه ثلاثاً, ولفظ البيهقي: جمروا كفن الميت ثلاثاً, وجميع يجمر فيه الميت ثلاثة مواضع: عند خروج روحه, وعند غسله, وعند تكفينه قوله: ولا يزاد على خمس ليس من الحديث وتبع فيه الزيلعي وزاد مثلا مسكين قوله أو سبعاً أفاده السيد قوله: (ولا) تتبع الجنازة بصوت ولا نار) كذا في حديث أبي داود وزاد في رواية ولا يمشي بين يديها قال محمد وبهذا نأخذ وهو قول أبي حنيفة قال في البدائع لأنه فعل أهل الكتاب فيكره التشبه بهم أي, ولأن فيه تفاؤلاً رديئاً
They used incense for the deceased and made it odd, and do not increase beyond five, and do not follow the funeral with sound or fire, and using incense for the grave is disliked. He said: (They used incense and made it odd) And in a narration of al-Hakim: When you use incense for the deceased, use it three times. And the wording of al-Bayhaqi: They used incense for the shroud of the deceased three times. And the deceased is fumigated in three places: when his soul departs, when he is washed, and when he is shrouded. He said: And do not increase beyond five is not from the hadith, and al-Zayla'i followed him and added 'or seven' as a possibility, which the Sayyid noted. He said: (And do not follow the funeral with sound or fire) as in the hadith of Abu Dawud, and in a narration: and do not walk in front of it. Muhammad said: with this we take, and it is the statement of Abu Hanifa. Al-Bada'i said: because it is the action of the People of the Book, so imitating them is disliked, and because it is an omen of evil.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the use of incense for the deceased and related prohibitions. The base text states that the deceased should be fumigated with incense in an odd number, not exceeding five. The commentary explains that the authentic hadith (from al-Hakim and al-Bayhaqi) specifies three times, at three moments: at death, during washing, and during shrouding. The number five or seven is not from the hadith, and is only a possibility noted by later scholars. The prohibition of following the funeral with sound (like wailing) or fire (like candles) is supported by a hadith in Abu Dawud. Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa hold that this is disliked because it resembles the practices of the People of the Book and involves pessimism. The dislike applies to the living followers, not to the deceased.

Key Terms
تجمير (tajmir): Fumigation with incense (bakhur) of the deceased or his shroud.
إيتار (itar): Making the number odd (witr), here referring to the number of incense burnings.
تفاؤل (tafa'ul): Pessimism or taking bad omens, which is disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on following a funeral procession with sound or fire according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is recommended
B) It is obligatory
C) It is disliked (makruh)
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
Use of incense for the deceased is sunnah in odd numbers, but following funerals with sound or fire is makruh according to the Hanafi school.

The Sufficiency Shroud and Rewards for Serving the Deceased
Base ruling on minimal shroud and hadiths on rewards

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the necessity shroud, identify the proof from the actions of the Prophet (ﷺ), and explain the rewards for washing, shrouding, and burying the deceased.

Core Idea
Covering the entire body of the deceased is obligatory; covering only the 'awrah is insufficient.

This lesson explores the minimal requirements for shrouding a deceased person in cases of necessity and the immense rewards promised for those who serve the dead. The Hanafi school, based on the actions of the Prophet (ﷺ) with Hamza and Mus'ab, holds that the entire body must be covered, unlike the Shafi'i position. Additionally, we examine the virtues of washing, shrouding, and digging the grave, and the etiquette of concealing faults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The ruling is about the shroud of necessity (kafan al-darurah): whatever cloth is available is sufficient, but it must cover the entire body. The passage also mentions rewards for washing, shrouding, and digging.
Focus on the legal ruling first.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the incident of Hamza and Mus'ab: the Prophet ordered Mus'ab's head to be covered and lemon grass placed on his feet, indicating the need to cover the whole body.
The hadith is cited as evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's command to cover the head and feet implies that covering only the private parts is insufficient; the entire body must be concealed.
This is the legal implication (wajh al-dalalah) from the action.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that covering the 'awrah suffices. The Hanafi school rejects this based on the same incident.
The dispute is mentioned explicitly in the text: 'khilafan lil-Shafi'i'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Al-Zayla'i and al-Tahtawi prefer the Hanafi position, and the mufta bihi is that the entire body must be covered.
The tarjih is based on the explicit command of the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وكفن الضرورة) للمرأة والرجل يكتفي فيه بكل ما يوجد) روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «من غسل ميتاً فكتم عليه غفر الله له أربعين كبيرة ومن كفنه كساه الله من السندس والاستبرق ومن حفر له قبراً حتى يجنه فكأنما أسكنه مسكناً حتى يبعث)، وورد بأعلى غسل الموتى قوله: (يكتفي فيه بكل ما يوجد) لما روي أن حمزة رضي الله عنه كفن في ثوب واحد، ومصعب بن عمير لم يوجد له شيء يكفن فيه إلا نمرة أي كساء فيه خطوط بيض وسود كما في المغرب، فكانت إذا وضعت على رأسه بدت رجلاه، وإذا وضعت على رجليه خرج رأسه فأمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يغطى رأسه ويجعل على رجليه شيء من الأذخر، وهذا دليل على أن ستر العورة وحدها لا يكفي خلافاً للشافعي كذا في الشرح عن الزيلعي إلا زيادة تفسير النمرة فمن السيد قوله: (حتى يجنه) أي يستره من أجن بمعنى ستر، وأفاد في القاموس أنه يأتي ثلاثياً، ورباعياً والجنن محركة القبر، وهذا الحديث رواه الحاكم في المستدرك وقال أنه على شرط مسلم، وفيه التصريح بأن هذا الفعل يكفر الكبائر، والظاهر أن محله أن كان بغير أجر، وقوله: فكتم عليه أي ستر عليه في الإزهار قال العلماء إذا رأى الغاسل من الميت ما يعجبه كاستنارة وجهه وطيب ريحه وسرعة انقلابه على المغتسل استحب يتحدث به وإن رأى ما يكره كنتنه وسواد وجهه وبدنه أو انقلاب صورته حرم أن يتحدث به كذا في شرح المشكاة قيل: إلا أن يكون مبتدعاً يظهر البدعة، أو مجاهر بالغسق، والظلم فيذكر ذلك زجراً لأمثاله كما في ابن أمير حاج، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من غسل ميتاً فليغتسل ومن حمله فليتوضأ رواه الإمام أحمد وأصحاب السنن إلا النسائي، والأمر فيه للندب، وصرفه عن الوجوب حديث ابن عباس المصرح فيه بعدم الوجوب قال محمد ونأخذ بأنه فإنه من غسل متياً غفر له سبعون مغفرة لو قسمت مغفرة منها على جميع الخلائق لوسعتهم قلت ما يقول من يغسل ميتاً قال: يقول: غفرانك يا رحمن حتى يفرغ من الغسل.
(And the shroud of necessity) for woman and man, one is content with whatever is available. It is narrated from the Prophet: 'Whoever washes a deceased and conceals his faults, Allah will forgive him forty major sins; whoever shrouds him, Allah will clothe him with silk and brocade; whoever digs a grave for him until he hides him, it is as if he housed him in a dwelling until the Resurrection.' And it is reported regarding the best of washing the dead. He said: (One is content with whatever is available) because it is narrated that Hamza was shrouded in one garment, and Mus'ab ibn 'Umayr had nothing to be shrouded in except a namira (a cloak with white and black stripes). When they placed it on his head, his feet appeared; when placed on his feet, his head came out. So the Prophet ordered that his head be covered and something of lemon grass be placed on his feet. This is evidence that covering the nakedness alone is not sufficient, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He said: (until he hides him) means covers him. This hadith is narrated by al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak and he said it meets Muslim's conditions. It explicitly states that this action expiates major sins, and apparently that is when done without payment. He said: (and conceals his faults) means covers them. Scholars said: if the washer sees something pleasing like illumination of face or good smell, he should speak of it; if he sees something disliked like stench or blackness, it is forbidden to speak of it. And the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever washes a deceased, let him perform ghusl; whoever carries him, let him perform wudu.' This is narrated by Ahmad and the authors of Sunan except al-Nasa'i. The command is for recommendation, not obligation, as per the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas. Muhammad said: we take that... For whoever washes a deceased, seventy pardons are granted, if one pardon were divided among all creation it would encompass them. I asked: what should the washer say? He said: Say: 'Your forgiveness, O Most Merciful' until he finishes the washing.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage combines a ruling on the minimal shroud and a collection of hadiths on the virtues of serving the deceased. The base rule is that in necessity, any available cloth suffices to cover the dead, even a single garment for a man or woman. The commentary cites the example of Hamza and Mus'ab to prove that covering the nakedness alone is insufficient; the entire body must be covered. This directly opposes the Shafi'i view that covering the nakedness is enough. Then follow hadiths on rewards: forty major sins forgiven for washing and concealing faults, clothing of Paradise for shrouding, and a dwelling in Paradise for digging the grave. The commentary clarifies that these rewards apply when done without payment. It also discusses the etiquette of the washer: to speak of good signs and conceal faults. Finally, there is a hadith about performing ghusl and wudu after handling the deceased, but the Hanafi position is that it is not obligatory (only recommended). The supplication during washing is also mentioned.

Key Terms
نمرة (namira): A striped cloak (white and black stripes) used as a shroud.
أذخر (idhkhir): Lemon grass, a fragrant plant, used to cover the feet of Mus'ab.
كتم (katama): To conceal, especially faults or defects of the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding covering a deceased in a shroud of necessity?
A) Only the private parts must be covered
B) Any available cloth that covers the entire body suffices
C) The shroud must be new and white
D) The shroud must be three garments

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the shroud must cover the entire body; the Prophet's actions with Mus'ab provide the decisive proof, and the rewards for serving the deceased are immense when done sincerely.

Praying Over Multiple Janazas: Individual vs Combined and Ordering
Ruling on individual vs combined janaza prayers and order of placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on combining multiple janazas in one prayer and explain the preferred order of placement according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Combining multiple janazas in one prayer is valid, but praying individually for each is better.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the Hanafi ruling when multiple funeral prayers coincide. While it is permissible to pray over all bodies in a single prayer, the preferred practice is to pray over each body individually. The passage also details the proper arrangement of bodies when they are combined, including the order of precedence (men, boys, khuntha, women) and the positioning relative to the imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
Recognize that the passage presents two possibilities: praying over each janaza separately (الإفراد) or combining them in one prayer (الجمع).
The preferred method is individual prayer, but combining is valid.
2
Determine the ruling (hukm) level
State that individual prayer is mustahabb (preferred) and combining is ja'iz (permissible). This is a ruling of preference, not obligation.
The mufta bihi is that individual prayer is afdal.
3
Distinguish the two methods of combining
When combining, one may arrange the bodies in a wide row (صف عريض) side by side, or in a long row (صف طويل) one behind the other. In the long row, the chest of each body must align with the imam.
The wide row has the imam stand at the chest of the best body; the long row aligns chests.
4
Learn the order of precedence
The bodies must be placed in a specific order based on gender and age: men nearest the imam, then boys, then khuntha (hermaphrodites), then women, then adolescent girls. Among men, the best and oldest goes first; a free man is preferred over a slave unless the slave is more righteous.
This order is observed when there is a difference in virtue (فضل).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on head positioning
Ibn Abi Layla held that in a long row, each body's head should be placed lower than the one before (like steps). Abu Hanifa said aligning heads is also good, citing the burial of the Prophet (saws) and his companions. The preferred view is to align chests, not heads.
The dispute does not affect validity, only optimal arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا اجتمعت الجنائز فالإفراد بالصلاة لكل منها أولى) وهو ظاهر ويقدم الأفضل فالأفضل إن لم يكن سبق (وإن اجتمعن ولو مع السبق (وصلى مرة) واحدة صح وإن شاء جعلهم صفاً عريضاً ويقوم عند أفضلهم وإن شاء (جعلها) أي الجنائز صفاً طويلاً مما يلي القبلة بحيث يكون صدر كل واحد منهم (قدام الإمام) محاذياً له، وقال ابن أبي ليلى يجعل رأس كل واحد أسفل من رأس صاحبه كذا درجات وقال أبو حنيفة هو حسن لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وصاحبيه دفنوا هكذا والوضع للصلاة كذلك قال : وإن وضعوا رأس كل واحد بحذاء رأس الآخر فحسن، وهذا كله عند التفاوت في الفضل، فإن لم يكن ينبغي أن لا يعدل عن المحاذاة فلذا قال : ( وراعى (الترتيب) في وضعهم (فيجعل الرجال مما يلي الإمام ثم الصبيان بعدهم) أي بعد الرجال (ثم الخناثي، ثم النساء) ثم المراهقات ولو كان الكل رجالاً. روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة يوضع أفضلهم، وأسنهم مما يلي الإمام وهو قول أبي يوسف والحر مقدم على العبد وفي رواية الحسن إذا كان العبد أصلح قدم
And if multiple funerals gather, then praying individually for each is better. And it is apparent, and the best and most virtuous (are) preferred, if there is no precedence. (And if they gather, even with precedence, and they pray once, it is valid. And if he wishes, he makes them a wide row and stands at the best of them. And if he wishes, he places them (i.e., the funerals) as a long row facing the qibla, such that the chest of each of them (is in front of the imam) aligned with him. Ibn Abi Layla said: place the head of each one lower than the head of his companion, like steps. Abu Hanifa said: that is good, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his two companions were buried like that, and the placement for prayer is similar. He said: and if they place the head of each one opposite the head of the other, that is good. And all this is when there is a difference in virtue. If not, they should not deviate from alignment. Therefore he said: (And he observes the order) in their placement, so he places the men next to the imam, then the boys after them, then the khuntha, then the women, then adolescent girls. And if all were men, according to Hasan from Abu Hanifa: the best and oldest is placed next to the imam; that is the saying of Abu Yusuf. A free man is given precedence over a slave; in a narration of Hasan, if the slave is more righteous, he is given precedence.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings when multiple janazas are present. The preferred method is to pray over each body individually, but if combined, it is valid. The imam may arrange them in a wide row (side by side) or a long row (one behind the other). In a long row, each body's chest should align with the imam. Ibn Abi Layla and Abu Hanifa differ on how the heads are positioned: Ibn Abi Layla says step-like (each head lower), Abu Hanifa says aligning heads is also good. The choice depends on differences in virtue. Additionally, when placing bodies, the order of precedence is: men, then boys, then khuntha (ambiguous), then women, then adolescent girls. Among men, the best and oldest should be nearest the imam; a free man is preferred over a slave unless the slave is more righteous.

Key Terms
خناثى (khuntha): Hermaphrodites; individuals with ambiguous gender.
فضل (fadl): Virtue or merit; determines positioning.
إفراد (ifrad): Praying over each janaza separately.
صف عريض (saff 'arid): Wide row; bodies placed side by side.
صف طويل (saff tawil): Long row; bodies placed one behind the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Men, then boys, then women, then khuntha.
B) Men, then boys, then khuntha, then women.
C) Women, then men, then boys, then khuntha.
D) Khuntha, then women, then boys, then men.

Key Takeaway
Praying individually over each janaza is mustahabb, but combining is valid; when combining, order by virtue and gender: men, boys, khuntha, women.

Washing a Deceased Non-Muslim Relative
Ruling on washing a deceased non-Muslim by his Muslim relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing a deceased non-Muslim relative, explain the minimal manner of washing, and cite the hadith of Abu Talib as proof.

Core Idea
Washing a non-Muslim relative is like washing a najis rag — permitted but not an act of worship.

When a non-Muslim relative dies and no non-Muslim guardian is present, a Muslim relative may handle the burial. But how should the body be treated? The Hanafi school distinguishes between ritual purification for Muslims and simple cleaning for non-Muslims. This lesson unpacks that distinction through the classic ruling from Maraqi al-Falah and its proof from the Prophet's instruction regarding Abu Talib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
Determine what act is being ruled upon and its legal classification. Here, the act is washing a deceased non-Muslim relative. The hukm is mubah (permissible), not wajib, because the deceased is not Muslim.
Permissibility is conditional on the absence of a non-Muslim guardian.
2
Understand the manner of washing
The text compares the washing to 'washing a najis rag' — meaning it is done solely to remove filth, not as an act of worship. Therefore, no sunan of ghusl (like wudu, beginning with the right, etc.) are observed. The body is treated as najis (if it falls into water, it contaminates it).
The goal is not to purify the deceased, but to serve the kinship tie.
3
Examine the details of shroud and burial
The shroud is minimal — just a rag, not the full sunnah shroud. The body is placed in a pit like a carcass, without a niche. Alternatively, the relative may hand the body over to the deceased's coreligionists and follow the funeral from a distance.
This reinforces that no Islamic burial honors are given.
4
Locate the dalil
The proof comes from the hadith of Ali about his father Abu Talib (a polytheist). The Prophet commanded Ali to bury his father and then to perform ghusl himself. The absence of instructions for washing the deceased indicates that minimal handling suffices.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it yields a zanni ruling, but it is relied upon in the madhhab.
5
Note the cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) also consider the non-Muslim's body najis, but they may differ on whether the Muslim must perform ghusl after touching the body. The Hanafi view is that only the affected area needs washing.
This difference affects practical fiqh in funeral arrangements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان لكافر قريب مسلم حاضر، ولا ولي له كافر غسله المسلم كغسل خرقة نجسة لا يراعي فيه سنة عامة في بني آدم ليكون حجة عليه لا تطهيراً له حتى لو وقع في ماء نجسه وكفنه في خرقة من غير مراعاة كفن السنة وألقاه في حفرة من غير وضع كالجيفة مراعاة لحق القرابة أو دفعه القريب إلى أهل ملته ويتبع جنازته من بعيد
And if a deceased non-Muslim has a Muslim relative present, and he has no guardian who is non-Muslim, the Muslim washes him like washing a najis rag; he does not observe the usual sunnah for the children of Adam (i.e. the deceased), in order for it to be a proof against him (the non-Muslim), not for his purification; so much so that if he (the corpse) falls into water, it becomes najis. He shrouds him in a rag without observing the funeral shroud of the Sunnah, and throws him into a pit without placing him (properly) like a carcass, out of consideration for kinship. Or the relative hands him over to his coreligionists and follows his funeral from afar.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on washing a deceased non-Muslim who has a Muslim relative. The Muslim relative is permitted to wash the body, but it is not considered an act of worship or purification. Rather, it is done as a service to the relative, like washing a najis cloth. Therefore, the washing does not follow the sunnah of ghusl for Muslims (e.g., no wudu, no beginning with right side). The body is treated as impure: if it falls into water, it contaminates it. The shroud is minimal — just a rag, no special kafan, no camphor or incense. The body is thrown into a pit like a carcass, without a niche or proper placement. Alternatively, the Muslim may hand the body over to the deceased's non-Muslim coreligionists, and follow the funeral from a distance. The proof comes from the hadith of Abu Talib, where the Prophet instructed Ali to wash his father (who died a polytheist) and then to perform ghusl himself. This shows the permissibility but also the lack of ritual honor.

Key Terms
غسل خرقة نجسة (ghusl kharqah najisah): Washing a najis rag: a type of washing that purifies the object from filth, not a ritual ghusl with intention and sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a non-Muslim dies and has a Muslim relative, what is the ruling on washing the body according to the Hanafi school?
A) The Muslim must perform full ritual ghusl on the body.
B) The Muslim may wash it like a najis cloth without observing sunnah.
C) The Muslim is prohibited from touching the body.
D) The body must be handed over to non-Muslims only.

Key Takeaway
Washing a deceased non-Muslim relative is permissible but not a ritual; it is done like cleaning a najis object, without observing Sunnah or intending purification.

Disposal of an Apostate's Body
Ruling on the treatment of an apostate's corpse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding the disposal of an apostate's body and explain the rationale based on the concept of milla.

Core Idea
The apostate's body is like a dog's carcass — no one may wash or bury it with any respect.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the disposition of an apostate who dies in apostasy. It establishes that because the apostate has no valid religion (milla), his body is not entitled to any funeral rites. The ruling is severe to underline the gravity of apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The treatment of the apostate's body. The ruling: no one may wash it; it is thrown into a pit.
2
Understand the legal cause ('illa)
The text gives the reason: 'because he has no milla (religion)'. What does 'no milla' imply? He is neither Muslim nor belonging to any recognized faith community.
3
Apply the simile
The body is compared to the carcass of a dog. This indicates complete disregard: no ritual washing, no prayer, no respect.
4
Note the exclusion of all people
The text says 'لا يمكن منه أحد' – no one is enabled to handle him. This includes both Muslims and non-Muslims, because apostasy places him outside all recognized religious communities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه إشارة إلى أن المرتد لا يمكن منه أحد لغسله لأنه لا ملة له فيلقى كجيفة كلب في حفرة
And in it is an indication that the apostate (murtadd) is not enabled for anyone to wash him, because he has no religion (milla), so he is thrown like the carcass of a dog into a pit.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage states the ruling regarding the body of an apostate (a Muslim who leaves Islam and dies in apostasy). The apostate is considered to have no valid religion (milla) — he is neither Muslim nor belonging to any recognized non-Muslim faith (ahl al-dhimma). Therefore, his body is not entitled to any funeral rites. No one — Muslim or non-Muslim — is permitted to wash him. Instead, his body is disposed of by being thrown into a pit like the carcass of a dog, without any form of respect or ritual. This is derived from the general principle that apostates are worse than original non-Muslims, as they rejected the truth after accepting it. The text also implies that if an apostate reverts to another religion (e.g., Christianity), he is still not handed over to them, as he is not considered a true follower of that religion in the sense of having a valid milla.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who was Muslim and then left Islam (apostate); in this context, he dies in apostasy.
كجيفة كلب (ka-jīfati kalb): Like the carcass of a dog: a simile indicating no respect, no ritual washing, and such disposal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the apostate's body not washed?
A) Because he is considered a non-believer and non-believers are not washed.
B) Because he has no religion (milla).
C) Because he died without repenting.
D) Because his family refuses to wash him.

Key Takeaway
The apostate's body is not entitled to any funeral rites because apostasy removes all religious affiliation, leaving him without a recognized milla.

Ruling on Using a Coffin and Spreading Dirt in the Grave
Ruling against using coffins and permissibility of spreading dirt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using a coffin, identify the exception for soft/damp ground, and explain the prohibition of placing bedding under the deceased.

Core Idea
A coffin is makruh unless the ground is soft or damp; the sunnah is the lahd, not the shaqq.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the Hanafi position on coffins and burial practices. While many assume a coffin is standard, the Hanafi school considers it disliked except when the ground requires it. The proof is the Prophet's hadith distinguishing the lahd for Muslims. Additionally, placing mattresses or pillows under the deceased is also disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and primary ruling
Locate the phrase 'ولا باتخاذ التابوت' to understand that the subject is using a coffin and the primary ruling is that it is not allowed (i.e., makruh).
The ruling applies even if the coffin is made of iron.
2
Identify the exception
Find the condition 'إذا كانت الأرض رخوة، أو ندية' (if the ground is soft or damp). In such cases, using a coffin is permissible and its cost is taken from the capital of the estate.
This exception is based on necessity.
3
Identify the proof
The hadith 'اللحد لنا والشق الغيرنا' is cited. Understand that the lahd (niche) is for Muslims and the shaqq (trench) is for others, implying that a coffin, which resembles the shaqq, is not the sunnah.
The hadith is ahad but accepted by consensus.
4
Identify the additional prohibition
The text also mentions that it is disliked to place a mattress, pillow, mat, or similar under the deceased. The sunnah is to spread dirt directly.
This reinforces simplicity in burial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا باتخاذ التابوت، ولو من حديد ويفرش فيه التراب لقوله : «اللحد لنا والشق الغيرنا» ويكون من رأس المال إذا كانت الأرض رخوة، أو ندية، ويكره التابوت في غيرها بإجماع العلماء. قوله : (ويقرش فيه التراب) ويكره أن يوضع تحت الميت في القبر مضربة أو مخدة، أو حصيرة أو نحو ذلك
And [it is not allowed] to take a coffin, even if made of iron, and dirt is spread in it because of his saying: 'The niche is for us and the trench is for others.' And it is from the capital if the ground is soft or moist, and the coffin is disliked in other [ground] by consensus of the scholars. His saying: 'and dirt is spread in it' and it is disliked to place under the deceased in the grave a mattress, or a pillow, or a mat, or the like.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on using coffins (tabut) and spreading dirt in the grave. The text begins by forbidding the use of a coffin (tabut) even if made of iron, based on the Prophet's hadith: 'The niche (lahd) is for us and the trench (shaqq) is for others.' This indicates that the sunnah is to bury in a niche (lahd) without a coffin. However, an exception is made when the ground is soft (rakhwa) or damp (nadiyya): in such cases, a coffin may be used, and its cost is taken from the capital of the estate. In all other types of ground, using a coffin is makruh (disliked) by consensus of the scholars. The commentator then notes that it is also disliked to place a mattress, pillow, mat, or similar item under the deceased in the grave.

Key Terms
التابوت (al-tabut): A coffin or box in which the deceased is placed for burial.
اللحد (al-lahd): A niche dug into the side of the grave towards the qibla, wherein the deceased is placed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to use a coffin (tabut) for burial according to this text?
A) Always permissible
B) Only when the ground is soft or damp
C) Only for women
D) Never permissible

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a coffin is disliked unless the ground is soft or damp; the sunnah is the lahd, and placing bedding under the deceased is also disliked.

Entering the Deceased into the Grave from the Qibla Side
Ruling on placing the deceased into the grave facing qibla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah method of placing the deceased into the grave and identify the preferred supplication to be recited.

Core Idea
Entering the deceased from the qibla side is sunnah; the burial supplication is a testimony of faith, not a prayer.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah details the recommended practice for placing the deceased into the grave. It emphasizes entering from the qibla side, following the Prophet's example, and reciting a specific supplication. Understanding this ruling helps ensure that burial rites align with the Hanafi sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act of entering the deceased into the grave is ruled as sunnah (recommended). The text uses the term 'أولى' (better) to indicate preference.
2
Distinguish the two methods
Compare idkhal (entering from qibla) with sal (sliding from the side). The text explicitly states that idkhal is better than sal.
Sal is permissible in necessity but less meritorious.
3
Analyze the supplication
Examine the wording 'باسم الله وعلى ملة رسول الله' as commanded by the Prophet. Understand from al-Sarakhsi's explanation that it is a testament of entrustment, not a prayer for forgiveness.
The phrase affirms the deceased's faith in Islam.
4
Apply the ruling in practice
When performing burial, ensure the body is placed from the qibla side, the carrier faces qibla, and the prescribed words are recited. This is the sunnah method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل الميت في القبر (من قبل القبلة) كما أدخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إن أمكن فتوضع الجنازة على القبر من جهة القبلة ويحمله الآخذ مستقبلاً حال الأخذ ويضعه في اللحد لشرف القبلة، وهو أولى من السل لأنه يكون ابتداء بالرأس، أو يكون بالرجلين (ويقول واضعه) في قبره كما أمر به النبي ، وكان يقوله إذا أدخل الميت القبر (باسم الله وعلى ملة رسول الله) قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : أي باسم الله وضعناك وعلى ملة رسول الله سلمناك، وفي الظهيرية إذا وضعوه قالوا باسم الله وبالله وفي الله وعلى ملة رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم).
And the deceased is entered into the grave from the direction of the qibla as the Prophet (peace be upon him) entered, if possible. So the bier is placed at the grave from the direction of the qibla, and the one taking [the deceased] carries him facing the qibla at the time of taking, and places him in the niche due to the honor of the qibla. This is better than the method of sliding (sal) because it begins with the head or with the feet. And the one placing him in his grave says as commanded by the Prophet, and he used to say when entering the deceased into the grave: 'In the name of Allah and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah.' Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: Meaning, in the name of Allah we have placed you, and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah we have entrusted you. And in al-Zahiriyya: when they place him they say: 'In the name of Allah, and with Allah, and in Allah, and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him).'
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage describes the sunnah method of placing the deceased into the grave. The deceased should be entered from the direction of the qibla, as the Prophet did, if possible. The bier is placed at the grave on the qibla side, and the person carrying the deceased faces the qibla while lowering him into the niche (lahd). This method (called idkhal) is preferred over the method of sliding (sal), in which the bier is placed beside the grave and the deceased is slid in from the side; the idkhal method begins with the head or feet. The one placing the deceased should say: 'Bismillah wa 'ala millati Rasulillah' (In the name of Allah and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah). Al-Sarakhsi explains that this means 'In the name of Allah we have placed you, and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah we have entrusted you.' Another version from al-Zahiriyya includes additional phrases: 'Bismillah wa billah wa fi Allah wa 'ala millati Rasulillah.' This is a recommended (mandub) act, not obligatory.

Key Terms
السل (al-sall): A method of placing the deceased into the grave by sliding him from the side, as opposed to entering from the qibla.
الملة (al-millah): Religion or creed; here it refers to the religion of Islam as brought by the Messenger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which method of placing the deceased into the grave is considered better (أولى)?
A) Sliding (sal) from the side
B) Entering from the qibla side (idkhal)
C) Lowering from the foot of the grave
D) Placing from above

Key Takeaway
The sunnah method of burial is to enter the deceased from the qibla side and recite the testimony of faith, affirming the deceased's religion.

Priority for Entering the Grave and Prohibition of Women
Ruling on grave entry hierarchy and prohibition of women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the priority order for who should enter a woman's grave and explain the Hanafi ruling that prohibits women from entering the grave.

Core Idea
A mahram relative has first priority to enter the grave; women are never to enter.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on who should enter the grave of a deceased woman. The text establishes a clear hierarchy, prioritizing mahram relatives, then others. It also absolutely prohibits women from entering the grave. The reasoning behind allowing non-mahram men to handle the body is derived from the principle that touching a foreign woman with a barrier is permissible in necessity during life, and the same applies after death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on who should enter the grave to place the deceased woman and who is prohibited from entering.
2
List the priority order
Note the hierarchy: mahram male relative, then non-mahram male relative, then righteous elderly men, then righteous young men.
The order is a recommendation, not obligation.
3
State the prohibition
Women are prohibited from entering the grave entirely, and may not remove the deceased.
This is an absolute prohibition, regardless of availability of men.
4
Trace the qiyas reasoning
The permissibility for non-mahram men to handle the body is based on qiyas: touching a foreign woman with a barrier during life is permissible in necessity, so after death it is also permissible.
The illa is necessity (darurah) and the presence of a barrier (ha'il).
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, arrange for mahram first; if unavailable, choose from the subsequent categories. Never allow women to enter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذو الرحم المحرم أولى بإدخال المرأة، ثم ذو الرحم غير المحرم، ثم الصالح من مشايخ جيرانها ثم الشبان الصلحاء ولا يدخل أحد من النساء القبر، ولا يخرجهن إلا الرجال، ولو كانوا أجانب لأنّ مس الأجنبي لها بحائل عند الضرورة جائز في حياتها فكذا بعد موتها
And the one who is a mahram relative is more entitled to enter the woman [into the grave], then the non-mahram relative, then the righteous elderly men among her neighbors, then the righteous young men. And none of the women should enter the grave, nor should they remove her except men, even if they are strangers, because touching a strange woman with a barrier in necessity is permissible in her life, so likewise after her death.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage establishes the hierarchy of who should enter the grave to place the deceased, specifically regarding a woman. The first priority is a mahram male relative (such as father, brother, son, etc.), followed by a non-mahram male relative (like a cousin), then righteous elderly men from her neighbors, and finally righteous young men. No woman should enter the grave, and men alone should handle the burial. The reasoning (qiyas) given is that touching a foreign woman through a barrier (like a cloth) is permissible during her life in necessity (e.g., medical treatment); therefore, it is also permissible after death for burial. This analogy supports the permissibility of strangers handling the deceased when no mahram is available, but women are completely excluded from entering the grave.

Key Terms
ذو الرحم المحرم (dhu al-rahim al-mahram): A male relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden, such as father, brother, son.
بحائل (bi-ha'il): With a barrier or covering, such as a cloth, that prevents direct skin contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who has the first priority to enter a woman into the grave?
A) A righteous young man
B) A mahram male relative
C) A non-mahram male relative
D) An elderly neighbor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling establishes a clear priority for who enters a woman's grave, with mahram relatives first, and absolutely prohibits women from entering.

6 min
Positioning the Deceased towards the Qibla
Ruling on positioning the deceased towards the qibla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on positioning the deceased towards the qibla, identify the proof, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between wajib and sunnah.

Core Idea
Facing the deceased to the qibla is a confirmed sunnah, not obligatory, so the grave is not exhumed after burial.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a key funerary practice: positioning the deceased on the right side facing the qibla. While the Prophet's command and a hadith establish this as a sunnah, some Hanafi scholars debated whether it is required (wajib). The author clarifies the relied-upon view and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its primary ruling
The act is placing the deceased on the right side facing the qibla. The primary ruling is based on the Prophet's command and the hadith from Abu Dawood.
This is a sunnah practice, not a pillar of burial.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Prophet's command and the hadith: 'البيت الحرام قبلتكم أحياء وأمواتاً' (The Sacred House is your qibla, alive and dead).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'قبلتكم أحياء وأمواتاً' extends the qibla ruling to the deceased, indicating they must face it.
This is a clear extension of a ruling from life to death.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis (e.g., al-Durr) consider it wajib, others (e.g., Ibn Amir Hajj) say sunnah. The text prefers sunnah because exhumation is haram if dirt is filled.
The practical consequence: if omitted after dirt is filled, leave as is.
5
Derive the consequence
If the deceased was placed incorrectly and dirt has not been poured, correct it; if dirt is filled, the grave should not be exhumed.
This ruling confirms the sunnah status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويوجه إلى القبلة على جنبه الأيمن بذلك أمر النبي الله ، وفي حديث أبي داود البيت الحرام قبلتكم أحياء وأمواتاً وتحل العقدة لأمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سمرة، وقد مات له ابن أطلق عقد رأسه وعقد رجليه ولأنه آمن من الانتشار
And he is turned to face the qibla on his right side; that is what the Prophet commanded. And in the hadith of Abu Dawood: 'The Sacred House is your qibla, alive and dead.' And the knot(s) are untied because of the Prophet's command to Samurah, when his son died, he loosened the knot of his head and tied his legs, because it is safer against being uncovered.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage discusses the position of the deceased in the grave: he should be turned to face the qibla on his right side. This is based on the Prophet's command, as well as the hadith from Abu Dawood that the Ka'bah is the qibla for both the living and the dead. The commentary also mentions untying the shroud knots (like the knot at the head and feet) based on the Prophet's instruction to Samurah when his son died. The reason given is that it is safer to prevent the shroud from becoming undone (intishar). There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars whether this positioning is wajib (obligatory) or sunnah. The text notes that if the deceased was placed incorrectly and dirt has not yet been filled, it should be corrected; if dirt has been filled, the grave should not be exhumed because the positioning is sunnah and exhumation is haram.

Key Terms
العقدة (al-‘uqda): The knot(s) of the shroud; untying them refers to loosening the head and foot ties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the deceased was placed not facing the qibla and dirt has already been filled, what should be done?
A) Exhume the grave and correct
B) Leave it as is because the positioning is sunnah
C) Only correct if the family demands
D) Recite Quran over the grave

Key Takeaway
Positioning the deceased towards the qibla on the right side is a confirmed sunnah; once the grave is filled, it should not be exhumed for this purpose.

Placing Bricks and Reeds over the Niche
Ruling on covering the niche (lahd) with bricks and reeds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended method for covering the grave niche (lahd) and cite the evidence from the Prophet's practice and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's works.

Core Idea
Placing bricks or reeds over the niche is sunnah; the Prophet led by example, and both materials are recommended.

In this passage, we learn the sunnah regarding the covering of the grave niche (lahd) to protect the deceased's face from falling soil. The Prophet himself placed bricks on his grave, and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in his authoritative works recommends both bricks and reeds. This lesson explores the evidence and practical application of this practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is covering the niche (lahd) with bricks or reeds. The ruling is sunnah (recommended).
Sunnah here means a recommended practice, not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet's practice: 'جعل على قبره اللبن' (He placed bricks on his grave). This is a primary evidence.
The dalil is a direct action of the Prophet.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action indicates recommendation (istihbab) because his actions are generally for guidance, not obligation, unless specified.
Hanafi usul: the Prophet's action is a proof of sunnah, not fard.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
No juristic dispute exists on this point among Hanafis; all agree it is recommended.
The evidence is clear and universally accepted.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Imam Muhammad's statements in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Asl confirm both bricks and reeds are recommended, settling any doubt.
These are authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسوّى (اللبن) بكسر الباء الموحدة واحده لبنة بوزن كلمة الطوب النيء ( عليه) أي على اللحد اتقاء لوجهه عن التراب لما . عليه الصلاة والسلام جعل على قبره ،اللبن، وروي طن من قصب بضم الطاء المهملة الحزمة ولا منافاة لا مكان الجمع بوضع اللبن منصوباً، ثم أكمل بالقصب وقال محمد في الجامع الصغير: (و) يستحب (القصب) واللبن وقال في الأصل: اللبن والقصب فدل
And the bricks (al-labin) – with kasra of the ba, its singular is labina on the weight of kalima – are placed level (upon the niche) to protect his face from dirt, because the Prophet placed bricks on his grave. And it is narrated: a bundle of reeds (qasab) – with damm of the ta – meaning a bundle, and there is no contradiction because it is possible to combine by placing bricks upright, then completing with reeds. And Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Reeds and bricks are recommended.' And he said in al-Asl: 'Bricks and reeds' so it indicates.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage discusses what should be placed over the niche (lahd) in the grave to prevent dirt from falling on the deceased. It recommends placing bricks (laban) or reeds (qasab). The text states that the Prophet himself placed bricks on his grave. A narration mentions a bundle of reeds (tun min qasab). There is no contradiction; one can place bricks first and then complete with reeds. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Jami' al-Saghir says both reeds and bricks are recommended (mustahabb), and in al-Asl he mentions bricks and reeds. This indicates the sunnah is to cover the niche with either or both materials. The purpose is to protect the face of the deceased from falling dirt. The number of bricks for the Prophet's grave was nine.

Key Terms
اللبن (al-labin): Unbaked bricks made of mud, square-shaped, used in construction.
القصب (al-qasab): Reeds or bamboo stalks placed over the niche to prevent dirt from falling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What material did Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani recommend for covering the niche in the grave?
A) Only bricks
B) Only reeds
C) Both bricks and reeds
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The sunnah is to cover the grave niche with bricks or reeds to protect the deceased's face, as taught by the Prophet and affirmed by Imam Muhammad.

6 min
Who May Enter the Grave: Priority and Prohibition of Women
Ruling on priority and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the priority order for entering the grave with a deceased woman and explain why women are prohibited from entering based on qiyas.

Core Idea
Women are prohibited from entering the grave; men may enter, with priority given to mahram relatives.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya establishes a clear hierarchy for who should enter the grave with a deceased woman and categorically prohibits women from entering. It also provides a qiyas (analogy) from life to justify non-mahram men handling the body with a barrier. Understanding this ruling is essential for proper funeral practices in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the priority order
List the four categories in order: mahram relative, non-mahram relative, elderly pious neighbor, pious young man.
Mahram = permanent marriage prohibition (e.g., father, brother).
2
Note the prohibition of women
The text explicitly says 'no woman should enter the grave' – this is a binding prohibition (haram) in the Hanafi school.
This applies even to female relatives.
3
Analyze the qiyas proof
The proof states: if touching a non-mahram woman with a barrier is permissible in necessity during life, it is also permissible after death for burial.
The 'illa (effective cause) is necessity (hajat).
4
Understand the condition of necessity
The permission is restricted to necessity (e.g., medical treatment, burial). It does not grant unrestricted touching.
Barrier (hail) must be present to avoid direct skin contact.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school allows women to enter graves if there is a need, based on general permissibility of visiting graves.
The Hanafi position is stricter, emphasizing prevention of fitna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذو الرحم المحرم أولى بإدخال المرأة، ثم ذو الرحم غير المحرم، ثم الصالح من مشايخ جيرانها ثم الشبان الصلحاء ولا يدخل أحد من النساء القبر، ولا يخرجهن إلا الرجال، ولو كانوا أجانب لأنّ مس الأجنبي لها بحائل عند الضرورة جائز في حياتها فكذا بعد موتها قوله : (ثم ذو الرحم غير المحرم المحرم غير ذي الرحم بمصاهرة أو رضاع مقدم عليه . قوله : (من مشايخ جيرانها قيل الشيخ من بلغ الثلاثين إلى الخمسين قوله : (ثم الشبان) هم من لم يبلغ السن المذكور. قوله : ( ولا يدخل أحد من النساء (القبر ولا كافر ولو كانا قريبين للميت ذكره ابن أمير حاج وفي نسخة بنصب أحد ولا وجه له إلا أن يجعل الفاعل ضميراً يعود على الولي مثلاً قوله ولا يخرجهن إلا (الرجال كذا في نسخة أي لا يخرجهن من الجنازة إلى القبر وكذا من المغتسل إلى السرير وفي نسخة ولا يخرجن والمعنى لا يخرجن إلى التشييع وتقدم ما فيه قوله : (عند الضرورة كالمداواة .
And the mahram male relative is more entitled to enter the woman [into the grave], then the non-mahram male relative, then the pious elders among her neighbors, then the pious young men. And no woman should enter the grave, and none should take them out [of the bier] except men, even if they are strangers, because touching a non-mahram woman with a barrier in necessity is permissible in her life, so likewise after her death. His statement: (then the non-mahram relative) - a non-mahram relative by marriage or fosterage is given precedence over him. His statement: (from the elders of her neighbors) - it is said the elder is one who has reached thirty to fifty. His statement: (then the young men) - they are those who have not reached that age. His statement: (and no one from the women should enter the grave) - nor a disbeliever, even if they are relatives of the deceased, as mentioned by Ibn Amir Hajj. In some copies it is 'nusiba ahad' but that has no basis unless the subject is a pronoun referring to the guardian. His statement: (and they are not taken out except by men) - as in some copies, meaning they are not taken out from the funeral to the grave, and likewise from the bath to the bier. In another copy: 'wa la yakhrujna' meaning they should not go out for accompanying. His statement: (in necessity like medical treatment).
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage discusses the etiquette and rulings for entering the grave during burial. The text first establishes a priority order for who should enter the grave with a deceased woman: first her mahram (a male relative she cannot marry), then a non-mahram relative, then elderly pious neighbors, and finally young pious men. The ruling for men is that any male may enter, but for women, it is prohibited for them to enter the grave entirely, even female relatives. The commentator explains that only men may remove the deceased from the bier and place her in the grave, and that a non-mahram touching a woman with a barrier is permissible in necessity (like medical treatment) in life, so by analogy it is allowed after death. The commentary clarifies terms: 'shaykh' here means age 30-50; 'shabban' means younger. It also notes that a non-relative by marriage or fosterage is preferred over a non-mahram relative. Women are prohibited from entering the grave, and also from accompanying the funeral procession (as per another copy) but this is discussed elsewhere. The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy) to life: if a necessity permits touching a non-mahram woman with a barrier in life, then after death it is also allowed for burial purposes. The authority weight is from the later Hanafi texts (fatawa mutakhkhirin) but rooted in the principle of necessity.

Key Terms
ذو الرحم المحرم (dhu al-rahim al-mahram): A male relative whom the deceased woman could not marry due to blood relationship, such as father, brother, son.
مس الأجنبي بها بحائل (mass al-ajnabi biha bi-hail): Touching a non-mahram woman with a barrier (like cloth) that prevents direct skin contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who is the first priority to enter the grave with a deceased woman?
A) Her husband
B) Her mahram male relative
C) Her non-mahram male relative
D) A pious young man from the neighborhood

Key Takeaway
Women are prohibited from entering the grave; mahram men have the highest priority, followed by non-mahram relatives and then pious men, with all handling requiring a barrier in necessity.

Directing the Deceased to the Qibla and Undoing the Knot
Ruling with proof on facing the deceased to qibla and untying knots

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on directing the deceased to the qibla and untying the shroud knots, and apply the correction rule when the direction is wrong before burial is sealed.

Core Idea
Facing the deceased to the qibla is wajib; correct before sealing the grave, do not exhume after burial.

When a Muslim passes away, specific rites must be performed for burial. This lesson covers two interrelated acts: positioning the deceased towards the qibla on the right side, and untying the knots of the shroud. The primary evidence comes from the Prophet's command and a hadith from Abu Dawud. Within the Hanafi school, there is a difference of opinion on whether the positioning is obligatory (wajib) or recommended (sunnah), with the relied-upon position being wajib. Additionally, the text provides a practical ruling on correcting a mistake in direction before and after the grave is sealed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being described (directing the deceased to the qibla on the right side, untying the knots). Note the primary ruling: according to the text, it is wajib (as per al-Durr) or sunnah (as per some narrations from Abu Hanifa).
The ruling is not unanimous within the school; be aware of the ikhtilaf.
2
Locate the evidence
Find the proofs the author cites: the command of the Prophet (ﷺ) to Ali regarding a deceased from Banu Abd al-Muttalib, and the hadith from Abu Dawud: 'The Sacred House of Allah is your qibla, alive and dead.' For untying the knots, the evidence is the Prophet's command to Samura when his son died.
These are ahadith transmissions, so their certainty (thubut) is zanni.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe the difference between al-Durr al-Mukhtar (wajib) and Ibn Amir Hajj reporting from Imam Abu Hanifa (sunnah). Understand that both opinions exist, but the mufta bihi (operative view) is wajib.
This ikhtilaf affects the sin of omission: if wajib, leaving it incurs sin; if sunnah, no sin but reward missed.
4
Apply the correction rule
If the deceased is placed incorrectly (e.g., not towards qibla or on the left side), the ruling depends on the state of the grave. If the bricks have been placed but earth not yet thrown, correct it. If earth has been thrown (burial complete), do not exhume because exhumation is haram and the act of positioning is only a sunnah (or wajib) but not a pillar that requires undoing the burial.
This practical rule balances between fulfilling a sunnah/wajib and preserving the sanctity of the grave.

Source Text

And he is directed towards the qibla on his right side; that is what the Prophet (ﷺ) commanded. And in the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'The Sacred House of Allah is your qibla, alive and dead.' And the knot is untied because of the command of the Prophet (ﷺ) to Samura, whose son died, so he loosened the knot of his head and the knot of his legs, and because it is safer from spreading. His statement: (and he is directed towards the qibla) – obligatory as in al-Durr, or recommended as in Ibn Amir Hajj from Imam (Abu Hanifa). So if he is placed other than the qibla or on his left side, then they remember, Imam said: If it is after placing the bricks but before the earth falls, they should change it and direct him to the qibla on his right; if they have already thrown the earth, the grave should not be exhumed because that is a sunnah and exhumation is haram. His statement: (that is what the Prophet commanded) – he commanded Ali when a man from Banu Abd al-Muttalib died, saying: O Ali, face him towards the qibla, and say all together 'In the name of Allah and on the religion of the Messenger of Allah', and place him on his side, and do not throw him on his face or on his back. His statement: (and the knot is untied) – and the one untying should say: 'O Allah, do not deprive us of its reward and do not try us after it.' His statement: (he loosened the knot of his head) – with a hamzah qat' open, and 'uqda' apparently with fatha on 'ayn and sukun on qaf as a verbal noun, not plural. His statement: (and the brick is placed) – with fatha on the lam in its singular and dual, and with kasra on the ba in both; some Arabs break the lam in both with sukun on ba like 'libda' and 'libad', and it is, as in al-Sihah, what is made from clay square and built with it.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage covers two actions: directing the deceased towards the qibla and undoing the knots of the shroud. The primary source is the command of the Prophet (ﷺ) as reported in hadith. The commentator notes a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: according to al-Durr al-Mukhtar, it is obligatory (wajib) to face the deceased to the qibla; according to Ibn Amir Hajj, it is only recommended (sunnah) as reported from Imam Abu Hanifa. If the deceased is placed incorrectly, the ruling depends on whether the grave has been sealed: if before the earth is thrown in, it can be corrected; if after, the grave should not be exhumed because exhuming is haram while the positioning is only a sunnah. The hadith from Abu Dawud is cited as proof: 'The Sacred House is your qibla, alive and dead.' The undoing of the knots is based on the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) who commanded Samura to undo the knots of his deceased son's shroud, and also for practical reasons to prevent the shroud from opening excessively. The commentator explains that the untying includes the knot at the head and the legs, and it is recommended to say a supplication. The passage also defines 'al-labin' (bricks) linguistically, which is a prelude to the next topic.

Key Terms
قبلة (qibla): The direction of the Ka'bah in Mecca, towards which Muslims pray and also for burial.
العقدة (al-‘uqda): The knot in the shroud that ties the shroud around the deceased's head or legs.
اللبن (al-labin): Bricks made of clay used to line the grave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is permissible if soon after burial
C) The grave should not be exhumed because it is haram
D) It must be done immediately

Key Takeaway
The deceased must be placed facing the qibla on the right side; if done wrong before burial, correct it; after burial, do not exhume.

Covering the Grave (Tasjiyah) and Throwing Earth
Ruling on covering a woman's grave and sunnah of three handfuls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on covering a woman's grave, explain the gender distinction, and apply the sunnah of throwing three handfuls of earth in burial.

Core Idea
Covering a grave is sunnah for women only; throwing three handfuls is sunnah for all.

This passage addresses two sunan (recommended acts) in burial: covering a woman's grave with a cloth to protect her modesty until the niche is filled, and throwing earth into the grave, especially three handfuls. The distinction between men and women is based on the action of Ali (RA). Understanding these rulings helps ensure burial customs align with the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the two separate sunan: covering the grave (tasjiyah) and throwing earth (hathw). Note their specific details.
2
Analyze the gender distinction for covering
Read the text regarding covering a woman's grave vs. not covering a man's, and note the exception for necessity (rain/snow). Identify the evidence from Ali (RA).
3
Understand the sunnah of throwing earth
Examine the recommendation of throwing three handfuls of earth and the basis in prophetic practice.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Review the difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars regarding covering a man's grave and the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) يستحب أن يسجى) أي يستر (قبرها) أي المرأة ستراً لها إلى أن يسوّى عليها اللحد (لا) يسجى (قبره) لأن علياً رضي الله عنه مر بقوم قد دفنوا ميتاً، وبسطوا على قبره ثوباً فجذب به وقال : إنما يصنع هذا بالنساء إلا إذا كان لضرورة دفع مطر، أو ثلج عن الداخلين في القبر، فلا بأس به (ويهال التراب ستراً له، ويستحب أن يحثى ثلاثاً لما أنه صلى على جنازة، قوله: (أن يسجى) بتشديد الجيم .مصباح قوله : إلى أن يستوى عليها اللحد) وفي المحيط إذا وضعت في اللحد استغني عن التسجية قهستاني قوله: (لا يسجى قبره) في الجلابي أصحابنا في تسجية قبره مختلفة منها ما يدل على الجواز، ومنها ما يدل على الكراهة قهستاني . قوله : (إنما يصنع هذا بالنساء هو آخر الأثر. قوله : (ويهال التراب) في القبر بالأيدي، وبالمساحي وبكل ما أمكن قوله : (ويستحب) أي لمن شهد دفن الميت أن يحثى في قبره
And it is recommended to cover (yasjaa) – meaning to cover (ustura) – her grave (qabraha), i.e., the woman's, as a covering for her until the earth is leveled over the niche; not to cover his grave (qabruhu). Because Ali (RA) passed by a group who had buried a dead person and spread a cloth over his grave, and he pulled it away and said: This is only done for women. His statement: (until the niche is leveled over her) – and in al-Muhit: if she is placed in the niche, covering is no longer needed (Qahastani). His statement: (not to cover his grave) – in al-Julabi: our associates have different rulings regarding covering his grave, some indicating permissibility, some indicating dislike (Qahastani). His statement: (this is only done for women) – this is the last of the athar. His statement: (and the earth is thrown) – into the grave with hands, shovels, and whatever is possible. His statement: (and it is recommended) – i.e., for whoever witnesses the burial of the deceased to throw into his grave
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage discusses two separate but connected sunnahs: (1) covering the grave of a woman with a cloth to protect her modesty until the niche is filled, and (2) throwing earth into the grave. For women, it is recommended to cover the grave with a veil-like cloth; for men, it is not done, based on the action of Ali (RA) who removed a cloth from a man's grave, stating that covering is only for women. However, if there is a necessity like rain or snow, it is permissible for men as well. There is ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars on whether covering a man's grave is permissible or disliked. The second sunnah is that earth is thrown into the grave, and it is recommended to throw three handfuls of earth, as this was the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) following a funeral prayer. The commentator clarifies that 'throwing' (hathw) can be done with hands or tools, and that the sunnah applies to all witnesses of the burial. The authority for covering women's graves is the athar (report) from Ali (RA), and the recommendation of three handfuls is based on prophetic practice (sunnah). This lesson combines a ruling on the distinction between men and women and a simple sunnah act for all burials.

Key Terms
التسجية (al-tasjiyah): Covering the grave of a deceased woman with a cloth until the niche is filled.
الحثو (al-hathw): Throwing earth into the grave; specifically, three handfuls (hathayath).

Dislike of Placing Baked Bricks and Wood in the Grave
Expanding on the karaha of bricks and wood in the grave with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which placing baked bricks and wood in the grave is disliked, and apply the ruling when necessity overrides the dislike.

Core Idea
Baked bricks and wood are disliked in graves only when simple alternatives exist and no genuine need requires them.

The base text of Maraqi al-Falah states that it is disliked to place baked bricks and wood in the grave. However, as with many Hanafi rulings, this general statement is refined by later commentators. In this passage, al-Tahtawi explains that the dislike is conditional: it depends on the availability of sun-dried brick (labin) and the intention behind the material. Understanding these conditions is essential to applying the ruling correctly in diverse circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is placing baked bricks (al-ajur) and wood in the grave. The ruling stated in the base text is karaha (dislike), which Hanafis classify as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in this context.
The base text's ruling is general; we will refine it in subsequent steps.
2
Locate the underlying reason (illa)
The commentator explains that the karaha is due to baked bricks and wood being used for buildings of judgment and adornment—things associated with worldly status and decoration. This conflicts with the simplicity and humility that graves should embody.
The illa is derived from qiyas: the general principle that graves should be simple.
3
Identify the condition for the karaha
The karaha applies specifically when sun-dried brick (labin) is available without cost. In such a case, using baked bricks or wood is a form of unnecessary extravagance.
Labin is the preferred material because it is simple and not associated with decoration.
4
Identify exceptions that remove the karaha
If there is a genuine need—such as protecting the grave from wild animals, preventing collapse, or when only rock is available—the karaha is lifted. The intention matters: if the material is used purely for adornment, the karaha remains; if for a practical purpose, it does not.
This is the critical refinement: the ruling is not absolute but conditional.
5
Refute the incorrect reason
The passage explicitly refutes the idea that the karaha is because the material has touched fire. That reasoning would incorrectly extend to other fire-touched items like shrouds or hot water, which are not disliked.
Stick to the correct illa: association with adornment and worldly status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذكور في الجامع على أنه لا بأس بالجمع بينهما واختلف في القصب المنسوج، ويكره إلقاء الحصير في القبر وهذا عند الوجدان، وفي محل لا يوجد إلا الصخر، فلا كراهة فيه فقولهم (وكره) وضع (الآجر) بالمد المحرق من اللبن (والخشب) محمول على وجود اللبن بلا كلفة، وإلا فقد يكون الخشب والآجر موجودين ويقدم اللبن لأن الكراهة لكونهما للأحكام والزينة، ولذا قال بعض مشايخنا : إنما يكره الآجر إذا أريد به الزينة أما إذا أريد به دفع أذى السباع، أو شيء آخر لا يكره وما قيل : أنه لمس النار فليس بصحيح
It is mentioned in al-Jami' that there is no harm in combining both. There is disagreement regarding woven reeds; it is disliked to throw a reed mat into the grave, and this is when there is availability. In a place where only rock exists, there is no dislike. So their statement 'it is disliked to place baked brick (al-ajur, with al-madd, meaning fired clay from sun-dried brick) and wood' is understood as when sun-dried brick is available without cost. Otherwise, wood and brick may be available but sun-dried brick is preferred, because the dislike is due to their being for judgments and adornment. Hence some of our scholars said: Brick is only disliked if it is intended for adornment, but if it is intended to repel the harm of wild animals, or something else, it is not disliked. And what is said that it is due to contact with fire is not correct.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hashiya, refining the base text's ruling from Maraqi al-Falah. The base text states that it is disliked to place baked bricks and wood in the grave. The commentator, Tahtawi, clarifies that this dislike is conditional: it applies when sun-dried brick (labin) is available without excessive cost. If only baked brick or wood is available, there is no dislike. The underlying reason (illa) for the karaha is that baked bricks and wood are associated with decorative buildings and judicial courts (symbols of authority), which conflicts with the humility of the grave. However, if there is a genuine need—such as to protect the grave from wild animals or to prevent the grave from collapsing—then there is no dislike. The commentator also refutes the idea that the karaha is due to the material having touched fire, as that would imply similar rulings for other fire-touched items like shrouds or hot water used in washing the deceased, which are not disliked.

Key Terms
الآجر (al-ajur): Baked bricks, fired from sun-dried clay (labin).
اللبن (al-labin): Sun-dried brick, not fired.
كراهة (karaha): Dislike, specifically prohibiting close to haram in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When sun-dried brick is available without cost
B) When the bricks are used to protect the grave from wild animals
C) When the bricks are brought from a demolished building
D) When the deceased was a judge or ruler

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on placing baked bricks and wood in the grave is not absolute; it depends on the availability of simpler alternatives and the intention, and it is lifted when genuine need exists.

Covering the Grave: A Ruling for Women, Not for Men
Ruling with proof from Hashiya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on covering the grave of a woman vs. a man, identify the evidence from 'Ali (RA), and apply the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
Covering a man's grave is not recommended; it is for women only, as stated by 'Ali (RA).

This lesson examines a specific ruling from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya: the recommendation to cover a woman's grave but not a man's. The ruling is based on a practice of 'Ali (RA) and balances modesty with simplicity. We will explore the proof, the gender distinction, and the exception for environmental necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'tusajja' (covering the grave). For a woman, it is mustahabb (recommended) to cover her grave until the lahd is sealed, to preserve modesty. For a man, it is not recommended, and doing so unnecessarily is makruh.
The ruling distinguishes based on gender, not general respect.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the action of 'Ali (RA), who removed a cloth from a man's grave and said: 'This is only done for women.' This is a qawl sahabi, considered a binding precedent in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but its meaning is unambiguous in this context.
3
Understand the exception
If there is a necessity like rain or snow that could disturb the grave or those inside, covering becomes permissible for both genders. This prevents harm.
The exception is limited to physical protection, not aesthetic or cultural reasons.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Jallabi) report a difference of opinion on covering the man's grave. However, the mufta bihi (operative position) follows the majority: gender distinction.
Non-Hanafi schools may differ; the ruling is mukhtalaf fih across madhahib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) يستحب أن تسجى) أي يستر (قبرها) أي المرأة ستراً لها إلى أن يسوّى عليها اللحد (لا) يسجى (قبره) لأن علياً رضي الله عنه مر بقوم قد دفنوا ميتاً، وبسطوا على قبره ثوباً فجذب به وقال : إنما يصنع هذا بالنساء إلا إذا كان لضرورة دفع مطر، أو ثلج عن الداخلين في القبر، فلا بأس به
And it is recommended that her grave be covered (i.e., the grave of a woman) as a covering for her until the lahd is levelled over her. His grave (the man's grave) is not covered, because 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, passed by a people who had buried a deceased person and spread a cloth over his grave; he pulled it away and said: This is only done for women. Except if there is a necessity to repel rain or snow from those entering the grave, then there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Hashiya. It states that it is recommended (mustahabb) to cover the grave of a woman with a cloth until the lahd is sealed, to preserve her modesty. The grave of a man should not be covered, based on the practice of 'Ali (RA) who removed a cloth from a man's grave and stated that covering is for women. However, if there is a necessity such as protecting the grave from rain or snow, covering is permissible for both genders. The commentary notes that the Hanafi school holds this view, though some later scholars have reported a difference of opinion on the matter. The ruling balances modesty for women with the general principle of simplicity in burial.

Key Terms
تسجى (tusajja): To cover the grave with a cloth or sheet.
اللحد (al-lahd): The niche dug in the side of the grave for placing the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because women's graves need more protection from the elements
B) Because 'Ali (RA) stated that covering is only for women
C) Because men are not required to be modest after death
D) Because women's burial involves additional rituals

Key Takeaway
Covering a woman's grave is recommended; covering a man's is not, based on 'Ali's statement, with an exception for rain or snow.
